<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the country in East Africa. </td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Legislature</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Tanzania)" title="National Assembly (Tanzania)">National Assembly</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2">Independence <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047488">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold">from the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and the <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German Empire</a></span></th></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">â¢Â <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(1961%E2%80%931964)" title="Tanganyika (1961â1964)">Tanganyika</a> </div></th><td class="infobox-data">9 December 1961</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">â¢Â <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Zanzibar" title="People's Republic of Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a></span> </div></th><td class="infobox-data">10 December 1963</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">â¢Â Merger </div></th><td class="infobox-data">26 April 1964</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">â¢Â Current constitution </div></th><td class="infobox-data">25 April 1977</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">

<p><b>Tanzania</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: secondary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/Ã¦/: 'a' in 'bad'">Ã¦</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/iË/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iË</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[b]</a></sup> <small>Swahili:Â </small><span class="IPA" lang="sw-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Swahili" title="Help:IPA/Swahili">[tanzaËni.a]</a></span>), officially the <b>United Republic of Tanzania</b> (<a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>: <i lang="sw">Jamhuri ya Muungano wa Tanzania</i>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> within the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region. It borders <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> to the north; <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> to the northeast; <a href="/wiki/Comoro_Islands" title="Comoro Islands">Comoro Islands</a> and the Indian Ocean to the east; <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> to the south; <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> to the southwest; and <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> to the west. <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a>, Africa's highest mountain, is in northeastern Tanzania. According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, Tanzania has a population of 63.59 million, making it the most populous country located entirely south of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>.
</p><p>Many important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominid</a> fossils have been found in Tanzania, such as 6-million-year-old <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a> hominid fossils. The genus <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">Australopithecus</a> ranged across Africa between 4 and 2 million years ago, and the oldest remains of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> are found near <a href="/wiki/Olduvai_Gorge" title="Olduvai Gorge">Lake Olduvai</a>. Following the rise of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> 1.8Â million years ago, humanity spread all over the Old World, and later in the New World and Australia under the species <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>. <i>H. sapiens</i> also overtook Africa and absorbed the older species of humanity.
</p><p>Later in the Stone and Bronze Age, prehistoric migrations into Tanzania included <a href="/wiki/South_Cushitic_languages" title="South Cushitic languages">Southern Cushitic</a> speakers who moved south from present-day Ethiopia;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup> Eastern Cushitic people who moved into Tanzania from north of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a> about 2,000 and 4,000 years ago;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Nilotic_languages" title="Southern Nilotic languages">Southern Nilotes</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Datooga_people" title="Datooga people">Datoog</a>, who originated from the present-day South SudanâEthiopia border region between 2,900 and 2,400 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 18">:âpage 18â</span></sup> These movements took place at about the same time as the settlement of the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Mashariki Bantu</a> from West Africa in the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a> areas. They subsequently migrated across the rest of Tanzania between 2,300 and 1,700 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-14">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>German rule began in mainland Tanzania during the late 19th century when Germany formed <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a>. This was followed by British rule after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. The mainland was governed as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(territory)" title="Tanganyika (territory)">Tanganyika</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Archipelago" title="Zanzibar Archipelago">Zanzibar Archipelago</a> remaining a separate colonial jurisdiction. Following their respective independence in 1961 and 1963, the two entities merged in 1964 to form the United Republic of Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-15">[13]</a></sup> The countries had joined the British <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> in 1961 and Tanzania is still a member of the Commonwealth as a unified republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tanzania's population is composed of about 120 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups" title="Ethnic groups">ethnic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-East_Africa_Living_Encyclopedia_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-East_Africa_Living_Encyclopedia-17">[15]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguistic_groups" title="Linguistic groups">linguistic</a>, and religious groups. The <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> of Tanzania is a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> constitutional republic and since 1996 its official capital city has been <a href="/wiki/Dodoma" title="Dodoma">Dodoma</a> where the president's office, the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Tanzania)" title="National Assembly (Tanzania)">National Assembly</a>, and all government ministries are located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a>, the former capital, retains most government offices and is the country's largest city, principal port, and leading commercial centre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-official_website_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-official_website-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> Tanzania is a <a href="/wiki/Dominant-party_system" title="Dominant-party system"><i>de facto</i> one-party state</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialist</a> <a href="/wiki/Chama_Cha_Mapinduzi" title="Chama Cha Mapinduzi">Chama Cha Mapinduzi</a> party in power.
</p><p>Tanzania is mountainous and densely forested in the north-east, where <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a> is located. Three of <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">Africa's Great Lakes</a> are partly within Tanzania. To the north and west lie <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>, Africa's largest lake, and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, the continent's deepest lake, known for its unique species of fish. To the south lies <a href="/wiki/Lake_Malawi" title="Lake Malawi">Lake Malawi</a>. The eastern shore is hot and humid, with the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> Archipelago just offshore. The <a href="/wiki/Menai_Bay_Conservation_Area" title="Menai Bay Conservation Area">Menai Bay Conservation Area</a> is Zanzibar's largest marine protected area. The <a href="/wiki/Kalambo_Falls" title="Kalambo Falls">Kalambo Falls</a>, located on the <a href="/wiki/Kalambo_River" title="Kalambo River">Kalambo River</a> at the Zambian border, is the second-highest uninterrupted <a href="/wiki/Waterfall" title="Waterfall">waterfall</a> in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalambo_Falls_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalambo_Falls-21">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> is the largest <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Tanzania" title="Religion in Tanzania">religion in Tanzania</a>, but there are also substantial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animist</a> minorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_Africa_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Africa-22">[20]</a></sup> Over 100 different <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Tanzania" title="Languages of Tanzania">languages are spoken in Tanzania</a>, making it the most linguistically diverse country in East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-sim-23">[21]</a></sup> The country does not have a <i>de jure</i> official language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mugoti_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mugoti-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-expogr_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-expogr-25">[23]</a></sup> although the national language is <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tzgov_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-tzgov-26">[24]</a></sup> Swahili is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentary" title="Parliamentary">parliamentary</a> debate, in the lower courts, and as a medium of instruction in primary school. English is used in foreign trade, in diplomacy, in higher courts, and as a medium of instruction in secondary and higher education;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-sim-23">[21]</a></sup> although the Tanzanian government is planning to discontinue English as the primary language of instruction, it will be available as an optional course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afkinsider.com_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-afkinsider.com-27">[25]</a></sup> Approximately 10% of Tanzanians speak Swahili as a first language, and up to 90% speak it as a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-sim-23">[21]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name "Tanzania" was created as a <a href="/wiki/Clipped_compound" title="Clipped compound">clipped compound</a> of the names of the two states that unified to create the country: <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(1961%E2%80%931964)" title="Tanganyika (1961â1964)">Tanganyika</a> and <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Zanzibar" title="People's Republic of Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> It consists of the first three letters of the names of the two states ("Tan" and "Zan") and the suffix, "ia" to form Tanzania.
</p><p>The name "Tanganyika" is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a> words <i>tanga</i> ("sail") and <i>nyika</i> ("uninhabited plain", "wilderness"), creating the phrase "sail in the wilderness". It is sometimes understood as a reference to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name of <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> comes from "<a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">zenji</a>", the name for a local people (said to mean "black"), and the Arabic word "barr", which means coast or shore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Tanzania" title="History of Tanzania">History of Tanzania</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Zanzibar" title="History of Zanzibar">History of Zanzibar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2625" data-file-width="2780" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg/240px-Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg/360px-Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg/480px-Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1.8-million-year-old stone chopping tool discovered at <a href="/wiki/Olduvai_Gorge" title="Olduvai Gorge">Olduvai Gorge</a> and on display at the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tanzania is one of the oldest continuously inhabited areas on Earth. Traces of fossil remains of humans and hominids date back to the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a> era. The <a href="/wiki/Olduvai_Gorge" title="Olduvai Gorge">Olduvai Gorge</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Ngorongoro_Conservation_Area" title="Ngorongoro Conservation Area">Ngorongoro Conservation Area</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>, features a collection with remnants of tools that document the development and use of transitional <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a>.
</p><p>The indigenous populations of eastern Africa are thought to be the <a href="/wiki/Hadza_language" title="Hadza language">linguistically isolated</a> <a href="/wiki/Hadza_people" title="Hadza people">Hadza</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandawe_people" title="Sandawe people">Sandawe</a> hunter-gatherers of Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 17">:âpage 17â</span></sup>
</p><p>The first wave of migration was by <a href="/wiki/South_Cushitic_languages" title="South Cushitic languages">Southern Cushitic</a> speakers who moved south from Ethiopia and Somalia into Tanzania. They are ancestral to the <a href="/wiki/Iraqw_language" title="Iraqw language">Iraqw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gorowa_language" title="Gorowa language">Gorowa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Burunge_language" title="Burunge language">Burunge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 17">:âpage 17â</span></sup> Based on linguistic evidence, there may also have been two movements into Tanzania of Eastern Cushitic people at about 4,000 and 2,000 years ago, originating from north of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: pages 17â18">:âpages 17â18â</span></sup>
</p><p>Archaeological evidence supports the conclusion that <a href="/wiki/Southern_Nilotic_languages" title="Southern Nilotic languages">Southern Nilotes</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Datooga_people" title="Datooga people">Datoog</a>, moved south from the present-day South Sudan / Ethiopia border region into central northern Tanzania between 2,900 and 2,400 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 18">:âpage 18â</span></sup>
</p><p>These movements took place at approximately the same time as the settlement of the iron-making <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Mashariki Bantu</a> from West Africa in the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a> areas. They brought with them the west African planting tradition and the primary staple of <a href="/wiki/Yam_(vegetable)" title="Yam (vegetable)">yams</a>. They subsequently migrated out of these regions across the rest of Tanzania between 2,300 and 1,700 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-14">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Nilotic_languages" title="Eastern Nilotic languages">Eastern Nilotic</a> peoples, including the <a href="/wiki/Maasai_people" title="Maasai people">Maasai</a>, represent a more recent migration from present-day South Sudan within the past 500 to 1,500 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics_13-9"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The people of Tanzania have been associated with the production of iron and steel. The <a href="/wiki/Pare_people" title="Pare people">Pare people</a> were the main producers of sought-after iron for peoples who occupied the mountain regions of north-eastern Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Haya_people" title="Haya people">Haya people</a> on the western shores of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> invented a type of high-heat <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>, which allowed them to forge <a href="/wiki/Carbon_steel" title="Carbon steel">carbon steel</a> at temperatures exceeding 1,820Â Â°C (3,310Â Â°F) more than 1,500 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Travellers and merchants from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and India have visited the east African coast since early in the first millennium AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> was practised by some on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a> as early as the eighth or ninth century A.D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval">Medieval</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Medieval">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bantu-speakers built farming and trade villages along the Tanzanian coast from the outset of the first millennium. Archaeological finds at <a href="/wiki/Fukuchani" title="Fukuchani">Fukuchani</a>, on the north-west coast of Zanzibar, indicate a settled agricultural and fishing community from the 6th century CE at the latest. The considerable amount of daub found indicates timber buildings, and shell beads, bead grinders, and iron slag have been found at the site. There is evidence for limited engagement in long-distance trade: a small amount of imported pottery has been found, less than 1% of total pottery finds, mostly from the Gulf and dated to the 5th to 8th century. The similarity to contemporary sites such as <a href="/wiki/Mkokotoni" title="Mkokotoni">Mkokotoni</a> and Dar es Salaam indicate a unified group of communities that developed into the first centre of coastal maritime culture. The coastal towns appear to have been engaged in Indian Ocean and inland African trade at this early period. Trade rapidly increased in importance and quantity beginning in the mid-8th century and by the close of the 10th century Zanzibar was one of the central Swahili trading towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Growth in Egyptian and Persian shipping from the Red Sea and the Persian Gulf revitalised <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade" title="Indian Ocean trade">Indian Ocean trade</a>, particularly after the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a> relocated to Fustat (Cairo). Swahili agriculturalists built increasingly dense settlements to tap into trade, these forming the earliest Swahili city-states. The Venda-Shona Kingdoms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mapungubwe" title="Mapungubwe">Mapungubwe</a> and Zimbabwe in South Africa and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, respectively, became a major producer of gold around this same period. Economic, social, and religious power was increasingly vested in <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Sultanate" title="Kilwa Sultanate">Kilwa</a>, Tanzania's major medieval city-state. Kilwa controlled a number of smaller ports stretching down to modern-day Mozambique. <a href="/wiki/Sofala" title="Sofala">Sofala</a> became the major gold emporium and Kilwa grew rich off the trade, lying at the southern end of the Indian Ocean Monsoons. Kilwa's major rivals lay to the north, in modern-day Kenya, namely Mombasa and Malindi. Kilwa remained the major power in East Africa until the arrival of the Portuguese at the end of the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial">Colonial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Colonial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_Territory" title="Tanganyika Territory">Tanganyika Territory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:City_of_Kilwa,_1572.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1443" data-file-width="1782" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg/220px-City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg/330px-City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg/440px-City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:City_of_Kilwa,_1572.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1572 depiction of the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilwa" title="Kilwa">Kilwa</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Claiming the coastal strip, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omani" title="Omani">Omani</a> Sultan <a href="/wiki/Said_bin_Sultan" title="Said bin Sultan">Said bin Sultan</a> moved his capital to <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_City" title="Zanzibar City">Zanzibar City</a> in 1840. During this time, Zanzibar became the centre for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_African_slave_trade" title="East African slave trade">east African slave trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> Between 65 and 90 per cent of the Arab-<a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> population of <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> was enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> One of the most infamous slave traders on the East African coast was <a href="/wiki/Tippu_Tip" title="Tippu Tip">Tippu Tip</a>, who was the grandson of an enslaved African. The <a href="/wiki/Nyamwezi_people" title="Nyamwezi people">Nyamwezi</a> slave traders operated under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Msiri" title="Msiri">Msiri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mirambo" title="Mirambo">Mirambo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Insoll" title="Timothy Insoll">Timothy Insoll</a>, "Figures record the exporting of 718,000 slaves from the Swahili coast during the 19th century, and the retention of 769,000 on the coast."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> In the 1890s, slavery was abolished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2584" data-file-width="3824" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg/220px-Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg/330px-Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg/440px-Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Battle during the <a href="/wiki/Maji_Maji_Rebellion" title="Maji Maji Rebellion">Maji Maji Rebellion</a> against <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German</a> colonial rule in 1905.</div></div></div>
<p>In the late 19th century, Germany conquered the regions that are now Tanzania (minus Zanzibar) and incorporated them into <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> (GEA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> The Supreme Council of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">1919 Paris Peace Conference</a> awarded all of GEA to Britain on 7 May 1919, over the strenuous objections of Belgium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ends_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 240">:â240â</span></sup> The British <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Secretary of State for the Colonies">colonial secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Milner,_1st_Viscount_Milner" title="Alfred Milner, 1st Viscount Milner">Alfred Milner</a>, and Belgium's minister <a href="/wiki/Plenipotentiary" title="Plenipotentiary">plenipotentiary</a> to the conference, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pierre_Orts&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pierre Orts (page does not exist)">Pierre Orts</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">Â [<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_Orts" title="fr:Pierre Orts">fr</a>]</span>, then negotiated the Anglo-Belgian agreement of 30 May 1919<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belgium_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belgium-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 618â9">:â618â9â</span></sup> where Britain ceded the north-western GEA provinces of <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Ruanda and Urundi</a> to Belgium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ends_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 246">:â246â</span></sup> The conference's Commission on Mandates ratified this agreement on 16 July 1919.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ends_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 246â7">:â246â7â</span></sup> The Supreme Council accepted the agreement on 7 August 1919.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belgium_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belgium-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 612â3">:â612â3â</span></sup> On 12 July 1919, the Commission on Mandates agreed that the small <a href="/wiki/Kionga_Triangle" title="Kionga Triangle">Kionga Triangle</a> south of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rovuma_River" title="Rovuma River">Rovuma River</a> would be given to <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Portuguese Mozambique</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ends_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 243">:â243â</span></sup> with it eventually becoming part of independent Mozambique. The commission reasoned that Germany had virtually forced Portugal to cede the triangle in 1894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ends_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 243">:â243â</span></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> was signed on 28 June 1919, although the treaty did not take effect until 10 January 1920. On that date, the GEA was transferred officially to Britain, Belgium, and Portugal. Also on that date, "Tanganyika" became the name of the British territory. In the mid-1920s, the British implemented a system of <a href="/wiki/Indirect_rule" title="Indirect rule">indirect rule</a> in Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Maji-Maji rebellion, between 1905 and 1907, was an uprising of several African tribes in German East Africa against the colonial authorities, in particular because of forced labor and deportation of certain tribes. It was the subject of a bloody repression, which combined with famine caused 300 000 deaths among the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, about 100,000 people from Tanganyika joined the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied forces</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heale_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heale-48">[46]</a></sup> and were among the 375,000 Africans who fought with those forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MGT_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-MGT-49">[47]</a></sup> Tanganyikans fought in units of the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_African_Rifles" title="King's African Rifles">King's African Rifles</a> during the <a href="/wiki/East_African_campaign_(World_War_II)" title="East African campaign (World War II)">East African Campaign</a> in Somalia and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Abyssinia</a> against the Italians, in Madagascar against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vichy_French" title="Vichy French">Vichy French</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Madagascar" title="Battle of Madagascar">Madagascar Campaign</a>, and in Burma against the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japanese</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma_Campaign_1944%E2%80%9345" title="Burma Campaign 1944â45">Burma Campaign</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MGT_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-MGT-49">[47]</a></sup> Tanganyika was an important source of food during this war, and its export income increased greatly compared to the pre-war years of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heale_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heale-48">[46]</a></sup> Wartime demand, however, caused increased commodity prices and massive inflation within the colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1954, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a> transformed an organisation into the politically oriented <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_African_National_Union" title="Tanganyika African National Union">Tanganyika African National Union</a> (TANU). TANU's main objective was to achieve national <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a> for Tanganyika. A campaign to register new members was launched, and within a year, TANU had become the leading political organisation in the country. Nyerere became Minister of British-administered Tanganyika in 1960 and continued as prime minister when Tanganyika became independent in 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>British rule came to an end on 9 December 1961. <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>, who had acceded to the British throne in 1952, continued to reign through the first year of Tanganyika's independence, but now distinctly as <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Tanganyika" title="Queen of Tanganyika">Queen of Tanganyika</a>, represented by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governor_general" title="Governor general">governor general</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 6">:âpage 6â</span></sup> Tanganyika also joined the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">British Commonwealth</a> in 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-16">[14]</a></sup> On 9 December 1962, Tanganyika became a democratic republic under an executive president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 6">:âpage 6â</span></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Revolution" title="Zanzibar Revolution">Zanzibar Revolution</a> overthrew the Arab dynasty in neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>, accompanied with the slaughter of thousands of Arab Zanzibaris,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar-53">[51]</a></sup> which had become independent in 1963, the archipelago merged with mainland Tanganyika on 26 April 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> The new country was then named the <i>United Republic of Tanganyika and Zanzibar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> On 29 October of the same year, the country was renamed the United Republic of Tanzania ("Tan" comes from Tanganyika and "Zan" from Zanzibar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-15">[13]</a></sup> The union of the two hitherto separate regions was controversial among many Zanzibaris (even those sympathetic to the revolution) but was accepted by both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyerere" title="Nyerere">Nyerere</a> government and the <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Government_of_Zanzibar" title="Revolutionary Government of Zanzibar">Revolutionary Government of Zanzibar</a> owing to shared political values and goals.
</p><p>Following Tanganyika's independence and unification with Zanzibar leading to the state of Tanzania, President Nyerere emphasised a need to construct a national identity for the citizens of the new country. To achieve this, Nyerere provided what is regarded as one of the most successful cases of ethnic repression and identity transformation in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> With over 130 languages spoken within its territory, Tanzania is one of the most ethnically diverse countries in Africa. Despite this obstacle, ethnic divisions remained rare in Tanzania when compared to the rest of the continent, notably its immediate neighbour, Kenya. Furthermore, since its independence, Tanzania has displayed more political stability than most African countries, particularly due to Nyerere's ethnic repression methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg/220px-Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg/330px-Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg/440px-Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Declaration_Monument" title="Arusha Declaration Monument">Arusha Declaration Monument</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1967, Nyerere's first presidency took a turn to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_politics" title="Left politics">left</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Declaration" title="Arusha Declaration">Arusha Declaration</a>, which codified a commitment to socialism as well as <a href="/wiki/Pan-Africanism" title="Pan-Africanism">Pan-Africanism</a>. After the declaration, banks and many large industries were nationalised.
</p><p>Tanzania was also aligned with China, which from 1970 to 1975 financed and helped build the 1,860-kilometre-long (1,160Â mi) <a href="/wiki/TAZARA_Railway" title="TAZARA Railway">TAZARA Railway</a> from Dar es Salaam to Zambia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> Nonetheless, from the late 1970s, Tanzania's economy took a turn for the worse, in the context of an international economic crisis affecting both developed and developing economies.
</p><p>In 1978, the neighboring Uganda, under the leadership of Idi Amin, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uganda-Tanzania_War" title="Uganda-Tanzania War">invaded Tanzania</a>. This disastrous invasion would culminate in Tanzania invading Uganda with the aid of Ugandan rebels and deposing Idi Amin as a result. However, the war severely damaged Tanzania's economy.
</p><p>Through the 1980s, conservation oriented national parks such as <a href="/wiki/Serengeti_National_Park" title="Serengeti National Park">Serengeti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kilimanjaro_National_Park" title="Kilimanjaro National Park">Kilimanjaro</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a> as the tallest freestanding summit on Earth, were included on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_List" title="UNESCO World Heritage List">UNESCO World Heritage List</a>.
</p><p>From the mid-1980s, the regime financed itself by borrowing from the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> and underwent some reforms. Since then, Tanzania's gross domestic product per capita has grown and poverty has been reduced, according to a report by the World Bank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1992, the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Tanzania" title="Constitution of Tanzania">Constitution of Tanzania</a> was amended to allow multiple political parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> In Tanzania's first multi-party elections, held in 1995, the ruling <a href="/wiki/Chama_Cha_Mapinduzi" title="Chama Cha Mapinduzi">Chama Cha Mapinduzi</a> won 186 of the 232 elected seats in the National Assembly, and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Mkapa" title="Benjamin Mkapa">Benjamin Mkapa</a> was elected as president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The presidents of Tanzania since Independence have been <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a>	1962â1985, <a href="/wiki/Ali_Hassan_Mwinyi" title="Ali Hassan Mwinyi">Ali Hassan Mwinyi</a> 1985â1995, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Mkapa" title="Benjamin Mkapa">Benjamin Mkapa</a>	1995â2005 <a href="/wiki/Jakaya_Kikwete" title="Jakaya Kikwete">Jakaya Kikwete</a> 2005â2015 <a href="/wiki/John_Magufuli" title="John Magufuli">John Magufuli</a> 2015â2021 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samia_Hassan_Suluhu" title="Samia Hassan Suluhu">Samia Hassan Suluhu</a> since 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> After the long tenure of president Nyerere, the Constitution has a term limit, a president can serve a maximum of two terms. Each term is five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> Every president has represented the ruling party Chama cha Mapinduzi (CCM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfr.org_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-cfr.org-65">[63]</a></sup> President Magufuli won a landslide victory and re-election in October 2020. According to the opposition, the <a href="/wiki/2020_Tanzanian_general_election" title="2020 Tanzanian general election">election</a> was full of fraud and irregularities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 17 March 2021, President <a href="/wiki/John_Magufuli" title="John Magufuli">John Magufuli</a> died from heart complications whilst in office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup> Magufuli's vice president, <a href="/wiki/Samia_Suluhu_Hassan" title="Samia Suluhu Hassan">Samia Suluhu Hassan</a>, became Tanzania's first female president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Tanzania" title="Geography of Tanzania">Geography of Tanzania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Archipelago" title="Zanzibar Archipelago">Zanzibar Archipelago</a></div>

href="/wiki/Ngorongoro_Crater" title="Ngorongoro Crater">Ngorongoro Crater</a>, the world's largest inactive and intact volcanic <a href="/wiki/Caldera" title="Caldera">caldera</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>At 947,303 square kilometres (365,756Â sqÂ mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFF_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-BFF-3">[3]</a></sup> Tanzania is the 13th largest country in Africa and the 31st largest in the world, ranked between the larger Egypt and smaller Nigeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Size_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Size-69">[67]</a></sup> It borders Kenya and Uganda to the north; Rwanda, Burundi, and the Democratic Republic of the Congo to the west; and Zambia, Malawi, and Mozambique to the south. Tanzania is located on the eastern coast of Africa and has an Indian Ocean coastline approximately 1,424 kilometres (885Â mi) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> It also incorporates several offshore islands, including <a href="/wiki/Unguja" title="Unguja">Unguja</a> (Zanzibar), <a href="/wiki/Pemba_Island" title="Pemba Island">Pemba</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mafia_Island" title="Mafia Island">Mafia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1245">:âpage 1245â</span></sup> The country is the site of Africa's highest and lowest points: <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a>, at 5,895 metres (19,341Â ft) above sea level, and the floor of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, at 1,471 metres (4,826Â ft) below sea level, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1245">:âpage 1245â</span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park,_Tanzania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1776" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg/230px-Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg/345px-Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg/460px-Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park,_Tanzania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wildebeest migration in the <a href="/wiki/Serengeti" title="Serengeti">Serengeti</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tanzania is mountainous and densely forested in the northeast, where Mount Kilimanjaro is located. Three of <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">Africa's Great Lakes</a> are partly within Tanzania. To the north and west lie <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>, Africa's largest lake, and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, the continent's deepest lake, known for its unique species of fish. To the southwest lies <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Nyasa" title="Lake Nyasa">Lake Nyasa</a>. Central Tanzania is a large plateau, with plains and arable land. The eastern shore is hot and humid, with the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Archipelago" title="Zanzibar Archipelago">Zanzibar Archipelago</a> just offshore.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kalambo_Falls" title="Kalambo Falls">Kalambo Falls</a> in the southwestern region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rukwa" title="Rukwa">Rukwa</a> is the second highest uninterrupted waterfall in Africa, and is located near the southeastern shore of Lake Tanganyika on the border with Zambia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalambo_Falls_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kalambo_Falls-21">[19]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Menai_Bay_Conservation_Area" title="Menai Bay Conservation Area">Menai Bay Conservation Area</a> is Zanzibar's largest marine protected area.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1366" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg/390px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg/585px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg/780px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg.png 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tanzania map of KÃ¶ppen climate classification</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Tanzania#Climate" title="Geography of Tanzania">Geography of Tanzania Â§Â Climate</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <p>Climate varies greatly within Tanzania. In the highlands, temperatures range between 10 and 20Â Â°C (50 and 68Â Â°F) during cold and hot seasons respectively. The rest of the country has temperatures rarely falling lower than 20Â Â°C (68Â Â°F). The hottest period extends between November and February (25â31Â Â°C or 77.0â87.8Â Â°F) while the coldest period occurs between May and August (15â20Â Â°C or 59â68Â Â°F). Annual temperature is 20Â Â°C (68.0Â Â°F). The climate is cool in high mountainous regions.
</p><p>Tanzania has two major rainfall periods: one is <a href="/wiki/Unimodality" title="Unimodality">uni-modal</a> (OctoberâApril) and the other is bi-modal (OctoberâDecember and MarchâMay).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rain_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-rain-72">[70]</a></sup> The former is experienced in southern, central, and western parts of the country, and the latter is found in the north from <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> extending east to the coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rain_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-rain-72">[70]</a></sup> The bi-modal rainfall is caused by the seasonal migration of the <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rain_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-rain-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Tanzania" title="Climate change in Tanzania">Climate change in Tanzania</a> is resulting in rising temperatures with a higher likelihood of intense rainfall events (resulting in flooding) and of dry spells (resulting in droughts).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> is already impacting the sectors in Tanzania of agriculture, water resources, health and energy. Sea level rise and changes in the quality of water are expected to impact fisheries and aquaculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tanzania produced a <a href="/wiki/National_Adaptation_Programme_of_Action" title="National Adaptation Programme of Action">National Adaptation Programmes of Action</a> (NAPAs) in 2007 as mandated by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a>. In 2012, Tanzania produced a National Climate Change Strategy in response to the growing concern of the negative impacts of climate change and climate variability on the country's social, economic and physical environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife_and_conservation">Wildlife and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wildlife and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Tanzania" title="Wildlife of Tanzania">Wildlife of Tanzania</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_protected_areas_of_Tanzania" title="List of protected areas of Tanzania">List of protected areas of Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mannetjes_masaigiraffe_in_de_Serengeti,_Tanzania,_-12_januari_2013_a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mannetjes_masaigiraffe_in_de_Serengeti%2C_Tanzania%2C_-12_januari_2013_a.jpg/220px-Mannetjes_masaigiraffe_in_de_Serengeti%2C_Tanzania%2C_-12_januari_2013_a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mannetjes_masaigiraffe_in_de_Serengeti%2C_Tanzania%2C_-12_januari_2013_a.jpg/330px-Mannetjes_masaigiraffe_in_de_Serengeti%2C_Tanzania%2C_-12_januari_2013_a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mannetjes_masaigiraffe_in_de_Serengeti%2C_Tanzania%2C_-12_januari_2013_a.jpg/440px-Mannetjes_masaigiraffe_in_de_Serengeti%2C_Tanzania%2C_-12_januari_2013_a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mannetjes_masaigiraffe_in_de_Serengeti,_Tanzania,_-12_januari_2013_a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a> is Tanzania's national animal</div></div></div>
<p>Tanzania contains around 20% of the species of Africa's enormous warm-blooded animal populace, found over its 21 National parks, reserves, 1 conservation area, and 3 marine parks. Spread over a zone of in excess of 42,000 square kilometres (16,000 sq. mi) and shaping around 38% of the nation's area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> Tanzania has 21 national parks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> plus a variety of game and forest reserves, including the <a href="/wiki/Ngorongoro_Conservation_Area" title="Ngorongoro Conservation Area">Ngorongoro Conservation Area</a>, however there so many people who live in Ngorongoro and try to affect the environment.  In western Tanzania, <a href="/wiki/Gombe_Stream_National_Park" title="Gombe Stream National Park">Gombe Stream National Park</a> is the site of <a href="/wiki/Jane_Goodall" title="Jane Goodall">Jane Goodall</a>'s ongoing study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_chimpanzee" title="Common chimpanzee">chimpanzee</a> behaviour, which started in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tanzania is highly <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiverse</a> and contains a wide variety of animal habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> On Tanzania's <a href="/wiki/Serengeti" title="Serengeti">Serengeti</a> plain, white-bearded <a href="/wiki/Wildebeest" title="Wildebeest">wildebeest</a> (<i>Connochaetes taurinus mearnsi</i>), other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bovid" title="Bovid">bovids</a> and zebra<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> participate in a large-scale annual migration. Tanzania is home to about 130 <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibian</a> and over 275 reptile species, many of them strictly <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> and included in the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a>'s Red Lists of countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> Tanzania has the largest lion population in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tanzania had a 2019 <a href="/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index" title="Forest Landscape Integrity Index">Forest Landscape Integrity Index</a> mean score of 7.13/10, ranking it 54th globally out of 172 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FLII-Supplementary_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FLII-Supplementary-85">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Tanzania" title="Politics of Tanzania">Politics of Tanzania</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Tanzania" title="Constitution of Tanzania">Constitution of Tanzania</a></div>
<p>Tanzania is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_party_dominant_state" title="One party dominant state">one-party dominant state</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chama_Cha_Mapinduzi" title="Chama Cha Mapinduzi">Chama Cha Mapinduzi</a> (CCM) party in power. From its formation until 1992, it was the only legally permitted party in the country. This changed on 1 July 1992, when the constitution was amended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 3">:âÂ§ 3â</span></sup> It has held power since independence in 1961, and is the longest-serving ruling party in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfr.org_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-cfr.org-65">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Magufuli" title="John Magufuli">John Magufuli</a> won the October 2015 presidential election and secured a two-thirds majority in parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> The main opposition party in Tanzania since multiparty politics in 1992 is called <a href="/wiki/Chadema" title="Chadema">Chama cha Demokrasia na Maendeleo (Chadema)</a> (<a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a> for "Party for Democracy and Progress"). The leader of Chadema party is <a href="/wiki/Freeman_Mbowe" title="Freeman Mbowe">Freeman Mbowe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Zanzibar, the country's semi-autonomous state, <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Change_and_Transparency" title="Alliance for Change and Transparency">The Alliance for Change and Transparency-Wazalendois</a> (ACT-Wazalendo) is considered the main opposition political party. The constitution of Zanzibar requires the party that comes in second in the polls to join a coalition with the winning party. ACT-Wazalendo joined a coalition government with the islands' ruling party Chama Cha Mapinduzi in December 2020 after Zanzibar disputed <a href="/wiki/2020_Zanzibari_general_election" title="2020 Zanzibari general election">elections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2020, Magufuli once again was declared the winner for his second term as president. Election fraud was suspected. The national electoral commission announced that Magufuli received 84%, or about 12.5 million votes and the top opposition candidate, <a href="/wiki/Tundu_Lissu" title="Tundu Lissu">Tundu Lissu</a> received 13%, about 1.9Â million votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2021, it was announced that Magufuli had died whilst serving in office, meaning that his vice president, <a href="/wiki/Samia_Suluhu_Hassan" title="Samia Suluhu Hassan">Samia Suluhu Hassan</a>, became the country's president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Executive">Executive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Executive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Samia_Suluhu_Hassan_in_May_2017.jpg/250px-Samia_Suluhu_Hassan_in_May_2017.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samia_Suluhu" title="Samia Suluhu">President Samia Suluhu Hassan</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr_Philip_Mpango.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="270" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dr_Philip_Mpango.jpg/150px-Dr_Philip_Mpango.jpg" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center"><a href="/wiki/Philip_Mpango" title="Philip Mpango">Vice-President Dr. Philip Mpango</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:112px;max-width:112px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kassim_Majaliwa.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1805" data-file-width="1333" decoding="async" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kassim_Majaliwa.jpg/165px-Kassim_Majaliwa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kassim_Majaliwa.jpg/220px-Kassim_Majaliwa.jpg 2x" width="110"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center"><a href="/wiki/Kassim_Majaliwa" title="Kassim Majaliwa">Prime Minister Kassim Majaliwa</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Tanzania" title="President of Tanzania">president of Tanzania</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Tanzania_National_Assembly_members" title="List of Tanzania National Assembly members">members of the National Assembly</a> are elected concurrently by direct popular vote for five-year terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 42(2)">:âÂ§ 42(2)â</span></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_Tanzania" title="Vice President of Tanzania">vice-president</a> is elected for a five-year term at the same time as the president and on the same ticket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§Â§ 47(2), 50(1)">:âÂ§Â§ 47(2),â50(1)â</span></sup> Neither the president nor the vice-president may be a member of the National Assembly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 66(2)">:âÂ§ 66(2)â</span></sup> The president appoints a <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Tanzania" title="Prime Minister of Tanzania">prime minister</a>, subject to confirmation by the assembly, to serve as the government's leader in the assembly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-4"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§Â§ 51(1)-(2), 52(2)">:âÂ§Â§ 51(1)-(2),â52(2)â</span></sup> The president selects her <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Tanzania" title="Cabinet of Tanzania">cabinet</a> from assembly members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-5"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 55">:âÂ§ 55â</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislature">Legislature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Legislature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislative</a> power relating to mainland Tanzania and union matters is vested in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Tanzania" title="National Assembly of Tanzania">National Assembly</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-6"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 64(1)">:âÂ§ 64(1)â</span></sup> which is unicameral and has a maximum of 357 members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edge_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edge-92">[90]</a></sup> These include members elected to represent constituencies, the attorney general, five members elected by the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> house of representatives from among its own members, the special women's seats that constitute at least 30% of the seats that any party has in the assembly, the speaker of the assembly (if not otherwise a member of the assembly), and the persons (not more than ten) appointed by the president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-7"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 66(1)">:âÂ§ 66(1)â</span></sup> The Tanzania Electoral Commission demarcates the mainland into constituencies in the number determined by the commission with the consent of the president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-8"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 75">:âÂ§ 75â</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judiciary">Judiciary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Judiciary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_Tanzania" title="Chief Justice of Tanzania">Chief Justice of Tanzania</a></div>
<p>Tanzania's legal system is based on English <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tanzania has a four-level judiciary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup> The lowest-level courts on the Tanzanian mainland are the Primary Courts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup> In Zanzibar, the lowest-level courts are the Kadhi's Courts for Islamic family matters and the Primary Courts for all other cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup> On the mainland, appeal is to either the District Courts or the Resident Magistrates Courts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-4"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup> In Zanzibar, appeal is to the Kadhi's Appeal Courts for Islamic family matters and the Magistrates Courts for all other cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-5"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup> From there, appeal is to the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Tanzania" title="High Court of Tanzania">High Court of Mainland Tanzania or Zanzibar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-6"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup> No appeal regarding Islamic family matters can be made from the High Court of Zanzibar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-7"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_Constitution_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar_Constitution-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 99(1)">:âÂ§ 99(1)â</span></sup> Otherwise, the final appeal is to the Court of Appeal of Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-8"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The High Court of mainland Tanzania has three divisions â commercial, labour, and land<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TZ_court_system_93-9"><a href="#cite_note-TZ_court_system-93">[91]</a></sup> â and 15 geographic zones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> The High Court of Zanzibar has an industrial division, which hears only labour disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mainland and union judges are appointed by the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_Tanzania" title="Chief Justice of Tanzania">Chief Justice of Tanzania</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> except for those of the <a href="/wiki/Appellate_court" title="Appellate court">Court of Appeal</a> and the High Court, who are appointed by the president of Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-9"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§Â§ 109(1), 118(2)â(3)">:âÂ§Â§ 109(1),â118(2)â(3)â</span></sup>
</p><p>Tanzania is a party to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rome_Statute_of_the_International_Criminal_Court" title="Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court">Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Zanzibar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spice_Islands_(Zanzibar_highlighted)_sv.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="879" data-file-width="713" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Spice_Islands_%28Zanzibar_highlighted%29_sv.svg/220px-Spice_Islands_%28Zanzibar_highlighted%29_sv.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Spice_Islands_%28Zanzibar_highlighted%29_sv.svg/330px-Spice_Islands_%28Zanzibar_highlighted%29_sv.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Spice_Islands_%28Zanzibar_highlighted%29_sv.svg/440px-Spice_Islands_%28Zanzibar_highlighted%29_sv.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spice_Islands_(Zanzibar_highlighted)_sv.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The semi-autonomous <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Archipelago" title="Zanzibar Archipelago">Zanzibar Archipelago</a></div></div></div>
<p>The legislative authority in Zanzibar over all non-union matters is vested in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Zanzibar)" title="House of Representatives (Zanzibar)">House of Representatives</a> (per the Tanzania constitution)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-10"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 106(3)">:âÂ§ 106(3)â</span></sup> or the Legislative Council (per the Zanzibar constitution).
</p><p>The Legislative Council has two parts: the president of Zanzibar and the House of Representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_86-11"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 107(1)-(2)">:âÂ§ 107(1)-(2)â</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_Constitution_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar_Constitution-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 63(1)">:âÂ§ 63(1)â</span></sup> The president is Zanzibar's head of government and the chairman of the Revolutionary Council, in which the executive authority of Zanzibar is invested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_Constitution_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar_Constitution-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§Â§ 5A(2), 26(1)">:âÂ§Â§ 5A(2),â26(1)â</span></sup> Zanzibar has two vice-presidents, with the first being from the main opposition party in the house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisa_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eisa-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Troubled_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Troubled-100">[98]</a></sup> The second is from the party in power and is the leader of government business in the House.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Troubled_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Troubled-100">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The president and the members of the House of Representatives have five-year terms and can be elected for a second term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_Constitution_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar_Constitution-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 28(2)">:âÂ§ 28(2)â</span></sup>
