</p><p>The president selects ministers from members of the House of Representatives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_Constitution_94-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar_Constitution-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 42(2)">:âÂ§ 42(2)â</span></sup> with the ministers allocated according to the number of House seats won by political parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisa_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eisa-99">[97]</a></sup> The Revolutionary Council consists of the president, both vice-presidents, all ministers, the <a href="/wiki/Attorney_general" title="Attorney general">attorney general</a> of Zanzibar, and other house members deemed fit by the president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisa_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eisa-99">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The House of Representatives is composed of elected members, ten members appointed by the president, all the regional commissioners of Zanzibar, the attorney general, and appointed female members whose number must be equal to 30 per cent of the elected members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_Constitution_94-5"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar_Constitution-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§Â§ 55(3), 64, 67(1)">:âÂ§Â§ 55(3),â64,â67(1)â</span></sup> The House determines the number of its elected members<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_Constitution_94-6"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar_Constitution-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 120(2)">:âÂ§ 120(2)â</span></sup> with the Zanzibar Electoral Commission determining the boundaries of each election constituency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_Constitution_94-7"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar_Constitution-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Â§ 120(1)">:âÂ§ 120(1)â</span></sup> In 2013, the House had 81 members: fifty elected members, five regional commissioners, the attorney general, ten members appointed by the president, and fifteen appointed female members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edge_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edge-92">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_subdivisions">Administrative subdivisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Administrative subdivisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Tanzania" title="Regions of Tanzania">Regions of Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Tanzania" title="Districts of Tanzania">Districts of Tanzania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subdivisions_of_Tanzania" title="Subdivisions of Tanzania">Subdivisions of Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tanzania,_administrative_divisions_-_de_-_colored_(%2Bdetails).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2947" data-file-width="3096" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tanzania%2C_administrative_divisions_-_de_-_colored_%28%2Bdetails%29.svg/220px-Tanzania%2C_administrative_divisions_-_de_-_colored_%28%2Bdetails%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tanzania%2C_administrative_divisions_-_de_-_colored_%28%2Bdetails%29.svg/330px-Tanzania%2C_administrative_divisions_-_de_-_colored_%28%2Bdetails%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tanzania%2C_administrative_divisions_-_de_-_colored_%28%2Bdetails%29.svg/440px-Tanzania%2C_administrative_divisions_-_de_-_colored_%28%2Bdetails%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tanzania,_administrative_divisions_-_de_-_colored_(%2Bdetails).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Regions of Tanzania</div></div></div>
<p>In 1972, local government on the mainland was abolished and replaced with direct rule from the central government. Local government, however, was reintroduced in the beginning of the 1980s, when the rural councils and rural authorities were re-established. Local government elections took place in 1983, and functioning councils started in 1984. In 1999, a Local Government Reform Programme was enacted by the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Tanzania)" title="National Assembly (Tanzania)">National Assembly</a>, setting "a comprehensive and ambitious agenda ... [covering] four areas: political decentralization, financial decentralization, administrative decentralization and changed central-local relations, with the mainland government having overriding powers within the framework of the Constitution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kunnat_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kunnat-101">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2016, Tanzania is divided into thirty-one <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Tanzania" title="Regions of Tanzania">regions</a> (<i>mkoa</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> twenty-six on the mainland and five in <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> (three on  <a href="/wiki/Unguja" title="Unguja">Unguja</a>, two on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pemba,_Tanzania" title="Pemba, Tanzania">Pemba</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> In 2012, the thirty former regions were divided into 169 <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Tanzania" title="Districts of Tanzania">districts</a> (wilaya), also known as local government authorities. Of those districts, 34 were urban units, which were further classified as three city councils (<a href="/wiki/Arusha" title="Arusha">Arusha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mbeya" title="Mbeya">Mbeya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mwanza" title="Mwanza">Mwanza</a>), nineteen municipal councils, and twelve town councils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012_census_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-2012_census-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The urban units have an autonomous city, municipal, or town council and are subdivided into wards and <i>mtaa</i>. The non-urban units have an autonomous district council but are subdivided into village councils or township authorities (first level) and then into <i>vitongoji</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kunnat_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kunnat-101">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The city of Dar es Salaam is unique because it has a city council whose area of jurisdiction overlaps three municipal councils. The mayor of the city council is elected by that council. The twenty-member city council is composed of eleven persons elected by the municipal councils, seven members of the National Assembly, and "Nominated members of parliament under 'Special Seats' for women". Each municipal council also has a mayor. "The City Council performs a coordinating role and attends to issues cutting across the three municipalities", including security and emergency services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> The city of Mwanza has a city council whose areal jurisdiction overlaps two municipal councils.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policies_and_partnerships">Foreign policies and partnerships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Foreign policies and partnerships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Tanzania" title="Foreign relations of Tanzania">Foreign relations of Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Embassy_of_Tanzania,_Washington,_D.C.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2064" data-file-width="1524" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Embassy_of_Tanzania%2C_Washington%2C_D.C.jpg/170px-Embassy_of_Tanzania%2C_Washington%2C_D.C.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Embassy_of_Tanzania%2C_Washington%2C_D.C.jpg/255px-Embassy_of_Tanzania%2C_Washington%2C_D.C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Embassy_of_Tanzania%2C_Washington%2C_D.C.jpg/340px-Embassy_of_Tanzania%2C_Washington%2C_D.C.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Embassy_of_Tanzania,_Washington,_D.C.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_Tanzania,_Washington,_D.C." title="Embassy of Tanzania, Washington, D.C.">Tanzanian Embassy</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_End,_Washington,_D.C." title="West End, Washington, D.C.">West End, Washington, D.C.</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Foreign Policies</b> of Tanzania are in process of review to replace the current New Foreign Policy of 2001, which was the first official foreign policy of Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaMinistry_NFP2_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FaMinistry_NFP2-107">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Citizen_22_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Citizen_22-108">[106]</a></sup> Before 2001, Tanzanian foreign policy was dictated by the various presidential declarations of Mwalimu Nyerere specifically Circular No. 2 of 1964,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaMinistry_about_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FaMinistry_about-109">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Declaration" title="Arusha Declaration">Arusha Declaration</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nyerere_67ad_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nyerere_67ad-110">[108]</a></sup> and Policy of Foreign Affairs of 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nyerere_67fa_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nyerere_67fa-111">[109]</a></sup> These declarations had focused foreign policy primarily on independence and sovereignty, human rights, and African unity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nzomo_99_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nzomo_99-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magoma_20_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Magoma_20-113">[111]</a></sup> The current New Foreign Policy of 2001 was established to better address the end of colonialism and the cold war, globalization, market economics and liberalization, and the multi-party state of Tanzania. Its primary focus is economic diplomacy and development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaMinistry_FP_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FaMinistry_FP-114">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>New Foreign Policy of 2001, which is still used today, has a foundation of 7 principles; <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Good_neighborliness" title="Good neighborliness">good neighborliness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pan-Africanism" title="Pan-Africanism">African unity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">non-alignment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_diplomacy" title="Economic diplomacy">economic diplomacy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/International_development" title="International development">global cooperation</a> for economic development and peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaMinistry_NFP_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FaMinistry_NFP-115">[113]</a></sup> The primary objectives are outlined as the protection and promotion of cultural and economic interests, establishment of relations with other nations driven by economic interest, economic self-sufficiency, internal and global peace, and regional political and economic integration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaMinistry_FP_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FaMinistry_FP-114">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaMinistry_NFP_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FaMinistry_NFP-115">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>A review of current foreign policy is being undertaken by the sixth phase government to replace the current New Foreign Policy of 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaMinistry_NFP2_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FaMinistry_NFP2-107">[105]</a></sup> Foreign Affairs Minister <a href="/wiki/Liberata_Mulamula" title="Liberata Mulamula">Liberata Mulamula</a> has stated the new policies will maintain the priority of and non-alignment of the 2001 policy while making additional top priorities the climate change and refocusing economic diplomacy with a greater focus on value-added exports and the digital economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Citizen_22_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Citizen_22-108">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_partnerships">International partnerships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: International partnerships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ictr_office_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1564" data-file-width="2289" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Ictr_office_3.jpg/220px-Ictr_office_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Ictr_office_3.jpg/330px-Ictr_office_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Ictr_office_3.jpg/440px-Ictr_office_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ictr_office_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_Rwanda" title="International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda">International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arusha" title="Arusha">Arusha</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:East_African_Legislative_Assembly.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/East_African_Legislative_Assembly.jpg/220px-East_African_Legislative_Assembly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/East_African_Legislative_Assembly.jpg/330px-East_African_Legislative_Assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/East_African_Legislative_Assembly.jpg/440px-East_African_Legislative_Assembly.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:East_African_Legislative_Assembly.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/East_African_Legislative_Assembly" title="East African Legislative Assembly">East African Legislative Assembly</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arusha" title="Arusha">Arusha</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tanzania is a member of many international organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations (UN)</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union (AU)</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community (EAC)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_African_Development_Community" title="Southern African Development Community">Southern African Development Community (SADC)</a> among many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirschler_19_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirschler_19-116">[114]</a></sup> Additionally, due to the strength of Tanzania's non-alignment, unity and internal peace since independence Tanzania frequently act as a mediator and location of treaties and agreements between other nations, such as the <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Agreement" title="Arusha Agreement">Arusha Agreement</a> with Europe, as well as the Arusha Accords with <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Accords_(Rwanda)" title="Arusha Accords (Rwanda)">Rwanda (1993)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Accords_(Burundi)" title="Arusha Accords (Burundi)">Burundi (2000)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chachage_04_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chachage_04-117">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waters_06_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waters_06-118">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Nations has a large current and historical presence in Tanzania and acts as an important partner in itself, and associated IGOs and NGOs, in many functions in the country, as well as functions based in Tanzania and implemented throughout the Great Lakes and Africa as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_tz_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_tz-119">[117]</a></sup> Of the many functions, the UN and Tanzania partner or the UN works with outside countries, most notably human rights and justice courts and reporting, education, development, climate change, health, and wildlife conservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_tzEntities_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_tzEntities-120">[118]</a></sup> While the UN primary offices are in <a href="/wiki/Oyster_Bay,_Tanzania" title="Oyster Bay, Tanzania">Oysterbay, Dar es Salaam</a>, many other offices, courts, and NGOs are based in <a href="/wiki/Arusha" title="Arusha">Arusha, TZ</a>. The most well-known example is the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_Rwanda" title="International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda">International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda</a> genocide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_SRES9771995_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_SRES9771995-121">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>The African Union consists of 55 nations in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AU_memStates_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-AU_memStates-122">[120]</a></sup> Tanzania is a founding member of the AU in 2001, and its predecessor the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity" title="Organisation of African Unity">Organisation of African Unity (OAU)</a> by the predecessors of Tanzania, <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(1961%E2%80%931964)" title="Tanganyika (1961â1964)">Tanganyika</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Zanzibar" title="Sultanate of Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>, in 1963. The Judicial Branch of the AU and its courts are located in Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AU_tzAgreement_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-AU_tzAgreement-123">[121]</a></sup> Originally the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Justice_of_the_African_Union" title="Court of Justice of the African Union">Court of Justice of the African Union</a>, it has been combined with the <a href="/wiki/African_Court_on_Human_and_Peoples%27_Rights" title="African Court on Human and Peoples' Rights">African Court on Human and Peoples' Rights</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/African_Court_of_Justice_and_Human_Rights" title="African Court of Justice and Human Rights">African Court of Justice and Human Rights (ACJHR)</a> which is located in Arusha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AU_tzAgreement_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-AU_tzAgreement-123">[121]</a></sup> Tanzania ratified and joined the AU-brokered <a href="/wiki/African_Continental_Free_Trade_Area" title="African Continental Free Trade Area">African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA)</a> on 17 January 2022, the largest free trade area in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agarwal_22_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agarwal_22-124">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The East African Community consisting of Tanzania, Uganda, Kenya, Rwanda, Burundi, South Sudan, and the Democratic Republic of Congo (DRC) is headquartered in Arusha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ugirashebuja_17_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ugirashebuja_17-125">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EAC_overview_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-EAC_overview-126">[124]</a></sup> Tanzania, along with Kenya and Uganda, is a founding member of the EAC in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EAC_history_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-EAC_history-127">[125]</a></sup> Following the German defeat in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> Tanzania joined the London-based <a href="/wiki/East_African_Currency_Board" title="East African Currency Board">East African Currency Board (EACB)</a> that was a customs union and provider of currency for Tanzania, Uganda, and Kenya from 1919 to 1948, the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_High_Commission" title="East Africa High Commission">East Africa High Commission (EAHC)</a> from 1948 to 1961, and East African Common Services Organization (EACSO) from 1961 to 1966. In 1966 all three countries formed the first East African Community which lasted until 1976, then the East African Co-operation from 1993 to 2000, before becoming reestablished as the East African Community in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EAC_history_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-EAC_history-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kadaga_22_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kadaga_22-128">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>The EAC has had a <a href="/wiki/Customs_union" title="Customs union">customs union</a> since 2005, with a free trade zone between member states and unified tariffs and trade agreements with non-member states and multinational organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ugirashebuja_17_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ugirashebuja_17-125">[123]</a></sup> The customs union also established a unified organization and sets of rules, such as <a href="/wiki/Rules_of_origin" title="Rules of origin">rules of origin</a>, for all trade within, into, and passing through member states. In 2010 a common market was established within the EAC for the free movement of labor, goods, people, capital, and services, as well as established rights of establishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ugirashebuja_17_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ugirashebuja_17-125">[123]</a></sup> The East African Monetary Union (EAMU) is proposed to be established in 2024 that will create a single common currency by the East African Central Bank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adam_17_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adam_17-129">[127]</a></sup> From the original reestablishment of the EAC, as laid out in Article 5(2) of the Treaty for the Establishment of the East African Community, the final goal for the EAC is always the political federation of all member states. In 2017 all member states adopted the <a href="/wiki/Confederation" title="Confederation">confederation</a> of EAC as a transitional precursor to the final federation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ugirashebuja_17_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ugirashebuja_17-125">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kadaga_22_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kadaga_22-128">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Southern African Development Community consists of 16 nations, including all countries of southern Africa plus Tanzania and the DRC from the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muntschick_17_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muntschick_17-130">[128]</a></sup> Tanzania is a founding member of SADC in 1994, as well as its predecessor the <a href="/wiki/Frontline_States" title="Frontline States">Frontline States (FLS)</a>, from 1960 to 1994. While FLS aimed to end apartheid, its successor SADC has the aims of furthering peace and security along with the economic and political integration of member states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muntschick_17_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Muntschick_17-130">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FIB-training-22_(9311333487).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="834" data-file-width="1250" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/FIB-training-22_%289311333487%29.jpg/220px-FIB-training-22_%289311333487%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/FIB-training-22_%289311333487%29.jpg/330px-FIB-training-22_%289311333487%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/FIB-training-22_%289311333487%29.jpg/440px-FIB-training-22_%289311333487%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FIB-training-22_(9311333487).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>FIB Tanzanian special forces during training</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tanzania_People%27s_Defence_Force" title="Tanzania People's Defence Force">Tanzania People's Defence Force</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tanzania_People%27s_Defence_Force" title="Tanzania People's Defence Force">Tanzania People's Defence Force</a> (TPDF) (Kiswahili: Jeshi la Wananchi wa Tanzania (JWTZ)) is the armed forces of Tanzania, operating as a people's force under civilian control. It is composed of five branches or commands: Land Force (army), Air Force, Naval Command, National Service, Headquarter (MMJ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> Tanzanian citizens are able to volunteer for military service from 15 years of age, and 18 years of age for compulsory National military service upon graduation from advanced secondary school. Conscript service obligation was 2 years as of 2004.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (October 2021)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Tanzania" title="Human rights in Tanzania">Human rights in Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_people_with_albinism" title="Persecution of people with albinism">Persecution of people with albinism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Tanzania" title="LGBT rights in Tanzania">LGBT rights in Tanzania</a></div>
<p>Throughout Tanzania, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_by_country_or_territory" title="LGBT rights by country or territory">sex acts between men</a> are illegal and carry a maximum penalty of life imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> According to a 2007 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Centre" title="Pew Research Centre">Pew Research Centre</a> survey, 95 percent of Tanzanians believed that homosexuality should not be accepted by society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>People with <a href="/wiki/Albinism" title="Albinism">albinism</a> living in Tanzania are often attacked, killed or mutilated because of superstitions related to the black-magical practice known as <a href="/wiki/Muti" title="Muti">muti</a> that say body parts of albinos have magical properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> Tanzania has the highest occurrence of this human rights violation among 27 African countries where <a href="/wiki/Muti" title="Muti">muti</a> is known to be practised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2019, <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> reported that the Tanzanian government annulled the right of NGOs as well as individuals to directly file any case against it at the Arusha-based African Court for Human and Peoples' Rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_infrastructure">Economy and infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Economy and infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Tanzania" title="Economy of Tanzania">Economy of Tanzania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poverty_in_Tanzania" title="Poverty in Tanzania">Poverty in Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tanzania_Product_Exports_(2019).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="715" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tanzania_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg/290px-Tanzania_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tanzania_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg/435px-Tanzania_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tanzania_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg/580px-Tanzania_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tanzania_Product_Exports_(2019).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A proportional representation of Tanzania exports, 2019</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GDP_per_capita_development_in_Tanzania.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/GDP_per_capita_development_in_Tanzania.svg/220px-GDP_per_capita_development_in_Tanzania.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/GDP_per_capita_development_in_Tanzania.svg/330px-GDP_per_capita_development_in_Tanzania.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/GDP_per_capita_development_in_Tanzania.svg/440px-GDP_per_capita_development_in_Tanzania.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GDP_per_capita_development_in_Tanzania.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Historical development of real GDP per capita in Tanzania, since 1950</div></div></div>
<p>As of 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, according to the IMF, Tanzania's gross domestic product (GDP) was an estimated $71 billion (nominal), or $218.5 billion on a <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> (PPP) basis. GDP per capita (PPP) was $3,574.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 2009 through to 2013, Tanzania's per capita GDP (based on constant local currency) grew an average of 3.5% per year, higher than any other member of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> (EAC) and exceeded by only nine countries in Sub-Saharan Africa: the Democratic Republic of the Congo, Ethiopia, Ghana, Lesotho, Liberia, Mozambique, Sierra Leone, Zambia, and Zimbabwe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tanzania's largest trading partners in 2017 for its US$5.3Â billion in exports were <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UND_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-UND-139">[137]</a></sup> Its imports totalled US$8.17Â billion, with India, Switzerland, Saudi Arabia, China, and the United Arab Emirates being the biggest partners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UND_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-UND-139">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tanzania weathered the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a>, which began in late 2008 or early 2009, relatively well. Strong gold prices, bolstering the country's <a href="/wiki/Mining_industry_of_Tanzania" title="Mining industry of Tanzania">mining industry</a>, and Tanzania's poor integration into global markets helped to insulate the country from the downturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1250">:âpage 1250â</span></sup> Since the recession ended, the Tanzanian economy has expanded rapidly thanks to strong tourism, telecommunications, and banking sectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1250">:âpage 1250â</span></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">United Nations Development Programme</a>, however, recent growth in the national economy has benefited only the "very few", leaving out the majority of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> Tanzania's 2013 <a href="/wiki/Global_Hunger_Index" title="Global Hunger Index">Global Hunger Index</a> was worse than any other country in the EAC except Burundi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHI_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-GHI-141">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 15">:âpage 15â</span></sup> The proportion of persons who were undernourished in 2010â12 was also worse than any other EAC country except Burundi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHI_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-GHI-141">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 51">:âpage 51â</span></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> declared the rise of the Tanzanian economy from low income to lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_income_country" title="Middle income country">middle income country</a>, as its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GNI_per_capita" title="GNI per capita">GNI per capita</a> increased from US$1,020 in 2018 to US$1,080 in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunger_and_poverty">Hunger and poverty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Hunger and poverty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Global Hunger Index ranked the situation as "alarming" with a score of 42 in the year 2000; since then the GHI has declined to 29.5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-globalhungerindex.org_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-globalhungerindex.org-144">[142]</a></sup> Children in rural areas suffer substantially higher rates of malnutrition and chronic hunger, although urban-rural disparities have narrowed as regards both stunting and underweight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanzania_UNDP_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tanzania_UNDP-145">[143]</a></sup> Low rural sector productivity arises mainly from inadequate infrastructure investment; limited access to farm inputs, extension services and credit; limited technology as well as trade and marketing support; and heavy dependence on rain-fed agriculture and natural resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanzania_UNDP_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tanzania_UNDP-145">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Approximately 68 per cent of Tanzania's 61.1Â million citizens live below the poverty line of $1.25 a day. 32 per cent of the population are malnourished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-globalhungerindex.org_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-globalhungerindex.org-144">[142]</a></sup> The most prominent challenges Tanzania faces in poverty reduction are unsustainable harvesting of its natural resources, unchecked cultivation, climate change and water- source encroachment, according to the United Nations Development Programme (UNDP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanzania_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tanzania-146">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are very few resources for Tanzanians in terms of credit services, infrastructure or availability to improved agricultural technologies, which further exacerbates hunger and poverty in the country according to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">UNDP.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanzania_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tanzania-146">[144]</a></sup> Tanzania ranks 159 out of 187 countries in poverty according to the United Nation's Human Development Index (2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanzania_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tanzania-146">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2019 <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> report showed that in the last 10 years, poverty has reduced by 8 percentage points, from 34.4% in 2007 to 26.4% in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup> A further report showed a reduction to 25.7% in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Tanzania" title="Agriculture in Tanzania">Agriculture in Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tea_fields,_Tukuyu,_Tanzania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Tea_fields%2C_Tukuyu%2C_Tanzania.jpg/220px-Tea_fields%2C_Tukuyu%2C_Tanzania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Tea_fields%2C_Tukuyu%2C_Tanzania.jpg/330px-Tea_fields%2C_Tukuyu%2C_Tanzania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Tea_fields%2C_Tukuyu%2C_Tanzania.jpg/440px-Tea_fields%2C_Tukuyu%2C_Tanzania.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tea_fields,_Tukuyu,_Tanzania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tea fields in Tukuyu</div></div></div>
<p>The Tanzanian economy is heavily based on agriculture, which in 2013 accounted for 24.5 per cent of gross domestic product,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 37">:âpage 37â</span></sup> provides 85% of exports,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-15">[13]</a></sup> and accounted for half of the employed workforce;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 56">:âpage 56â</span></sup> The agricultural sector grew 4.3 per cent in 2012, less than half of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millennium_Development_Goal" title="Millennium Development Goal">Millennium Development Goal</a> target of 10.8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup> 16.4 per cent of the land is <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup> with 2.4 per cent of the land planted with <a href="/wiki/Permanent_crop" title="Permanent crop">permanent crops</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> Tanzania's economy relies on farming, but climate change has impacted their farming.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Maize_production_in_Tanzania" title="Maize production in Tanzania">Maize</a> was the largest food crop on the Tanzania mainland in 2013 (5.17Â million tonnes), followed by cassava (1.94Â million tonnes), sweet potatoes (1.88Â million tonnes), beans (1.64Â million tonnes), bananas (1.31Â million tonnes), rice (1.31Â million tonnes), and millet (1.04Â million tonnes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 58">:âpage 58â</span></sup> Sugar was the largest <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crop</a> on the mainland in 2013 (296,679 tonnes), followed by cotton (241,198 tonnes), cashew nuts (126,000 tonnes), tobacco (86,877 tonnes), coffee (48,000 tonnes), sisal (37,368 tonnes), and tea (32,422 tonnes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-5"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 58">:âpage 58â</span></sup> Beef was the largest meat product on the mainland in 2013 (299,581 tonnes), followed by lamb/mutton (115,652 tonnes), chicken (87,408 tonnes), and pork (50,814 tonnes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-6"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 60">:âpage 60â</span></sup>
</p><p>According to the 2002 National Irrigation Master Plan, 29.4Â million hectares in Tanzania are suitable for irrigation farming; however, only 310,745 hectares were actually being irrigated in June 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Industry.2C_energy_and_construction"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry,_energy_and_construction">Industry, energy and construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Industry, energy and construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Tanzania" title="Energy in Tanzania">Energy in Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Tanzania" title="Water supply and sanitation in Tanzania">Water supply and sanitation in Tanzania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource_and_waste_management_in_Tanzania" title="Natural resource and waste management in Tanzania">Natural resource and waste management in Tanzania</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_companies_of_Tanzania" title="List of companies of Tanzania">List of companies of Tanzania</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Williamson_Diamond_Mine,_Mwazui,_Tanzania.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Williamson_Diamond_Mine%2C_Mwazui%2C_Tanzania.JPG/200px-Williamson_Diamond_Mine%2C_Mwazui%2C_Tanzania.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Williamson_Diamond_Mine%2C_Mwazui%2C_Tanzania.JPG/300px-Williamson_Diamond_Mine%2C_Mwazui%2C_Tanzania.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Williamson_Diamond_Mine%2C_Mwazui%2C_Tanzania.JPG/400px-Williamson_Diamond_Mine%2C_Mwazui%2C_Tanzania.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Williamson_diamond_mine" title="Williamson diamond mine">Williamson diamond mine</a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg/200px-Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg/300px-Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg/400px-Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Songo_Songo_Island" title="Songo Songo Island">Songo Songo</a> Gas Plant</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Industry and construction is a major and growing component of the Tanzanian economy, contributing 22.2 per cent of GDP in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-7"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 37">:âpage 37â</span></sup> This component includes mining and quarrying, manufacturing, electricity and natural gas, water supply, and construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-8"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 37">:âpage 37â</span></sup> Mining contributed 3.3 per cent of GDP in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-9"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 33">:âpage 33â</span></sup> The vast majority of the country's mineral export revenue comes from gold, accounting for 89 per cent of the value of those exports in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-10"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 71">:âpage 71â</span></sup> It also exports sizeable quantities of gemstones, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamonds" title="Diamonds">diamonds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanzanite" title="Tanzanite">tanzanite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-4"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1251">:âpage 1251â</span></sup> All of Tanzania's coal production, which totalled 106,000 short tons in 2012, is used domestically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIA_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIA-153">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Only 15 per cent of Tanzanians had access to electric power in 2011, rising to 35.2 per cent in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-data.worldbank.org_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-data.worldbank.org-154">[152]</a></sup> The government-owned <a href="/wiki/TANESCO" title="TANESCO">Tanzania Electric Supply Company Limited</a> (TANESCO) dominates the electric supply industry in Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[c]</a></sup> The country generated 6.013Â billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hours" title="Kilowatt hours">kilowatt hours</a> (kWh) of electricity in 2013, a 4.2 per cent increase over the 5.771Â billion kWh generated in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BudEx_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-BudEx-158">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 4">:âpage 4â</span></sup> Generation increased by 63 per cent between 2005 and 2012;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012Elec_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-2012Elec-159">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup> Almost 18 per cent of the electricity generated in 2012 was lost because of theft and transmission and distribution problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012Elec_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-2012Elec-159">[156]</a></sup> The electrical supply varies, particularly when droughts disrupt hydropower electric generation; rolling blackouts are implemented as necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-5"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1251">:âpage 1251â</span></sup> The unreliability of the electrical supply has hindered the development of Tanzanian industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-6"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1251">:âpage 1251â</span></sup> In 2013, 49.7 per cent of Tanzania's electricity generation came from natural gas, 28.9 per cent from hydroelectric sources, 20.4 per cent from thermal sources, and 1.0 per cent from outside the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BudEx_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-BudEx-158">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 5">:âpage 5â</span></sup> The government has built a 532 kilometres (331Â mi) gas pipeline from <a href="/wiki/Mnazi_Bay-Ruvuma_Estuary_Marine_Park" title="Mnazi Bay-Ruvuma Estuary Marine Park">Mnazi Bay</a> to Dar es Salaam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[158]</a></sup> This pipeline was expected to allow the country to double its electricity generation capacity to 3,000 megawatts by 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup> The government's goal is to increase capacity to at least 10,000 megawatts by 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg/220px-Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg/330px-Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg/440px-Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nyerere Bridge in Kigamboni, Dar es Salaam</div></div></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/PFC_Energy" title="PFC Energy">PFC Energy</a>, 25 to 30Â trillion cubic feet of recoverable natural gas resources have been discovered in Tanzania since 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIA_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-EIA-153">[151]</a></sup> bringing the total reserves to over 43Â trillion cubic feet by the end of 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[161]</a></sup> The value of natural gas actually produced in 2013 was US$52.2Â million, a 42.7 per cent increase over 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-11"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 73">:âpage 73â</span></sup>
</p><p>Commercial production of gas from the Songo Songo Island field in the Indian Ocean commenced in 2004, thirty years after it was discovered there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EABrief_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-EABrief-165">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWURA-Ng_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWURA-Ng-166">[163]</a></sup> Over 35Â billion cubic feet of gas was produced from this field in 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-12"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 72">:âpage 72â</span></sup> with proven, probable, and possible reserves totalling 1.1Â trillion cubic feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWURA-Ng_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-EWURA-Ng-166">[163]</a></sup> The gas is transported by pipeline to Dar es Salaam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EABrief_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-EABrief-165">[162]</a></sup> As of 27 August 2014, TANESCO owed the operator of this field, Orca Exploration Group Inc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>A newer natural gas field in Mnazi Bay in 2013 produced about one-seventh of the amount produced near Songo Songo Island<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-13"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 73">:âpage 73â</span></sup> but has proven, probable, and possible reserves of 2.2Â trillion cubic feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWURA-Ng_166-2"><a href="#cite_note-EWURA-Ng-166">[163]</a></sup> Virtually all of that gas is being used for electricity generation in <a href="/wiki/Mtwara" title="Mtwara">Mtwara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EABrief_165-2"><a href="#cite_note-EABrief-165">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ruvuma and Nyuna regions of Tanzania have been explored mostly by the discovery company that holds a 75 per cent interest, Aminex, and has shown to hold in excess of 3.5Â trillion cubic feet of natural gas. A pipeline connecting offshore natural gas fields to Tanzania's commercial capital Dar es Salaam was completed at the end of April 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Tanzania" title="Tourism in Tanzania">Tourism in Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG/220px-Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG/330px-Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG/440px-Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The snowcapped <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uhuru_Peak" title="Uhuru Peak">Uhuru Peak</a></div></div></div>
<p>Travel and tourism contributed 17.5 per cent of Tanzania's gross domestic product in 2016<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-169">[166]</a></sup> and employed 11.0 per cent of the country's labour force (1,189,300 jobs) in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knoema_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knoema-170">[167]</a></sup> Overall receipts rose from US$1.74Â billion in 2004 to US$4.48Â billion in 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knoema_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-Knoema-170">[167]</a></sup> and receipts from international tourists rose from US$1.255Â billion in 2010 to US$2Â billion in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-169">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup> In 2016, 1,284,279 tourists arrived at Tanzania's borders compared to 590,000 in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UND_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-UND-139">[137]</a></sup> The vast majority of tourists visit <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> or a "northern circuit" of <a href="/wiki/Serengeti_National_Park" title="Serengeti National Park">Serengeti National Park</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ngorongoro_Conservation_Area" title="Ngorongoro Conservation Area">Ngorongoro Conservation Area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarangire_National_Park" title="Tarangire National Park">Tarangire National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Manyara_National_Park" title="Lake Manyara National Park">Lake Manyara National Park</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-7"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1252">:âpage 1252â</span></sup> In 2013, the most visited national park was Serengeti (452,485 tourists), followed by Manyara (187,773) and Tarangire (165,949).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abstract_52-14"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page xx">:âpage xxâ</span></sup>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banking">Banking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Banking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Tanzania" title="Bank of Tanzania">Bank of Tanzania</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central bank</a> of Tanzania and is primarily responsible for maintaining price stability, with a subsidiary responsibility for issuing the banknotes and coins of the <a href="/wiki/Tanzanian_shilling" title="Tanzanian shilling">Tanzanian shilling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup> At the end of 2013, the total assets of the Tanzanian banking industry were <a href="/wiki/Tanzanian_shilling" title="Tanzanian shilling">TShÂ </a>19.5Â trillion, a 15 per cent increase over 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Tanzania" title="Transport in Tanzania">Transport in Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg/220px-Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg/330px-Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg/440px-Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the main trunk roads</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="690" data-file-width="1198" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg/220px-Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg/330px-Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg/440px-Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zanzibar harbour</div></div></div>
<p>Most transport in Tanzania is by road, with road transport constituting over 75 per cent of the country's freight traffic and 80 per cent of its passenger traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-8"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1252">:âpage 1252â</span></sup> The 86,500 kilometres (53,700Â mi) road system is in generally poor condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-9"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1252">:âpage 1252â</span></sup> Tanzania has two railway companies: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TAZARA" title="TAZARA">TAZARA</a>, which provides service between Dar es Salaam and <a href="/wiki/Kapiri_Mposhi" title="Kapiri Mposhi">Kapiri Mposhi</a> (in a copper-mining district in Zambia), and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania_Railways_Limited" title="Tanzania Railways Limited">Tanzania Railways Limited</a>, which connects Dar es Salaam with central and northern Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-10"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1252">:âpage 1252â</span></sup> Rail travel in Tanzania often entails slow journeys with frequent cancellations or delays, and the railways have a deficient safety record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-11"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1252">:âpage 1252â</span></sup>
</p><p>In Dar es Salaam, there is a huge project of rapid buses, <a href="/wiki/Dar_Rapid_Transit_Agency" title="Dar Rapid Transit Agency">Dar Rapid Transit (DART)</a> which connects suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a> city. The development of the DART system consists of six phases and is funded by the African Development Bank, the World Bank and the Government of Tanzania. The first phase began in April 2012, and it was completed in December 2015 and launched operations in May 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tanzania has four international airports, along with over 120 small airports or landing strips. Airport infrastructure tends to be in poor condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-12"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1253">:âpage 1253â</span></sup> Airlines in Tanzania include <a href="/wiki/Air_Tanzania" title="Air Tanzania">Air Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Precision_Air" title="Precision Air">Precision Air</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fastjet" title="Fastjet">Fastjet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coastal_Aviation" title="Coastal Aviation">Coastal Aviation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ZanAir" title="ZanAir">ZanAir</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-13"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1253">:âpage 1253â</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">Communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Communications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Tanzania" title="Telecommunications in Tanzania">Telecommunications in Tanzania</a></div>
<p>In 2013, the communications sector was the fastest growing in Tanzania, expanding 22.8 per cent; however, the sector accounted for only 2.4 per cent of gross domestic product that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BudEx_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-BudEx-158">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 2">:âpage 2â</span></sup>
</p><p>As of 2011, Tanzania had 56 mobile telephone subscribers per 100 inhabitants, a rate slightly above the sub-Saharan average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-14"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1253">:âpage 1253â</span></sup> Very few Tanzanians have fixed-line telephones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-15"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1253">:âpage 1253â</span></sup> Approximately 12 per cent of Tanzanians used the internet as of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, though this number is growing rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-16"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1253">:âpage 1253â</span></sup> The country has a fibre-optic cable network that replaced unreliable satellite service, but internet bandwidth remains very low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-17"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1253">:âpage 1253â</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_supply_and_sanitation">Water supply and sanitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Water supply and sanitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Tanzania" title="Water supply and sanitation in Tanzania">Water supply and sanitation in Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa,_2012_or_closest_year.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="336" data-file-width="232" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg/220px-Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg/330px-Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg/440px-Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa,_2012_or_closest_year.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Domestic expenditure on research in Southern Africa as a percentage of GDP, 2012 or closest year. Source: UNESCO Science Report: towards 2030 (2015), Figure 20.3</div></div></div>
<p>Water supply and sanitation in Tanzania has been characterised by decreasing access to <a href="/wiki/Improved_water_source" title="Improved water source">improved water sources</a> in the 2000s (especially in urban areas), steady access to some form of sanitation (around 93 per cent since the 1990s), intermittent water supplies, and generally low quality of service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mowi_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-mowi-175">[172]</a></sup> Many utilities are barely able to cover their operation and maintenance costs through revenues because of low tariffs and poor efficiency. There are significant <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Tanzania" title="Regions of Tanzania">regional</a> differences, with the best performing utilities being <a href="/wiki/Arusha" title="Arusha">Arusha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moshi,_Tanzania" title="Moshi, Tanzania">Moshi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tanga,_Tanzania" title="Tanga, Tanzania">Tanga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pubexp_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-pubexp-176">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>The government of Tanzania has embarked on a major sector reform process since 2002. An ambitious National Water Sector Development Strategy that promotes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integrated_Water_Resources_Management" title="Integrated Water Resources Management">Integrated Water Resources Management</a> and the development of urban and rural water supply was adopted in 2006. Decentralisation has meant that responsibility for water and sanitation service provision has shifted to local government authorities and is carried out by 20 urban utilities and about 100 district utilities, as well as by Community Owned Water Supply Organisations in rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mowi_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-mowi-175">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>These reforms have been backed by a significant increase of the budget starting in 2006, when the water sector was included among the priority sectors of the National Strategy for Growth and Reduction of Poverty. The Tanzanian water sector remains heavily dependent on external donors, with 88 per cent of the available funds being provided by external donor organisations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWSDS_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWSDS-177">[174]</a></sup> Results have been mixed. For example, a report by <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Gesellschaft_f%C3%BCr_Internationale_Zusammenarbeit" title="Deutsche Gesellschaft fÃ¼r Internationale Zusammenarbeit">Deutsche Gesellschaft fÃ¼r Internationale Zusammenarbeit</a> noted that "despite heavy investments brought in by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, (the utility serving <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a>) has remained one of the worst performing water entities in Tanzania."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIZ_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIZ-178">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_statistics_controversy">Economic statistics controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Economic statistics controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two articles in the <i>Economist</i> in July 2020 raised doubts about official claims of economic growth: "If Tanzania's economy grew by almost 7% in the fiscal year to the end of June 2019, why did tax revenue fall by 1%? And why has bank lending to companies slumped? Private data are bad, too. In 2019 sales at the biggest brewer fell by 5%. Sales of cement by the two biggest producers were almost flat. None of these things is likely if growth is storming ahead. The discrepancies are so large that it is hard to avoid the conclusion that the government is lying."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tim Staermose, a proponent of African investment, took issue with these data: "Some of these statements by <i>The Economist</i>, based on the evidence I have gathered from primary sources â namely, the statutory financial reports that listed companies in Tanzania are legally obligated to release â are simply not true. Bank lending to companies as far as I can see has not, 'slumped.' The two biggest banks in Tanzania, which between them account for approximately 40% of the banking sector, both reported strong loan growth in 2019. ... As for cement sales being 'almost flat,' again, this is total nonsense. ... In 2019 Twiga sold 6% more cement by volume than it did in 2018. In the first six months of 2020, Twiga already sold 8% more cement than it had done by the same stage in 2019. Again, these numbers are very consistent with an economy that's reported to be growing at around 7% per annum. ... [On] the 5% fall in beer sales in 2019 ... the published 2019 annual report by Tanzania Breweries Limited (TBL) will tell you there were one-off circumstances that largely drove the decline ... [which] resulted in sales falling. But TBL's profits actually rose in 2019."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_and_nutrition">Food and nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Food and nutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pilua_for_many.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3610" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Pilua_for_many.jpg/220px-Pilua_for_many.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Pilua_for_many.jpg/330px-Pilua_for_many.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Pilua_for_many.jpg/440px-Pilua_for_many.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pilua_for_many.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Tanzanian woman cooks Pilau rice dish wearing traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanga_(African_garment)" title="Kanga (African garment)">Kanga</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Poor nutrition remains a persistent problem within Tanzania and varies hugely throughout the country's regions. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">USAID</a> reports that 16% of children are underweight and 34% experience stunted growth as a result of malnutrition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-182">[179]</a></sup> 10 regions house 58% of children suffering from stunted growth while 50% of acutely malnourished children can be found in 5 regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-183">[180]</a></sup> Over a 5-year period, the <a href="/wiki/Mara_Region" title="Mara Region">Mara</a> district of Tanzania saw a 15% reduction in stunting in children under 5 years old, falling from 46% to 31% in 2005 and 2010 respectively. <a href="/wiki/Dodoma" title="Dodoma">Dodoma</a>, on the other hand, saw a 7% increase in the prevalence of stunting in this age group, rising from 50% in 2005 to 57% in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-184">[181]</a></sup> Overall availability of food does not necessarily contribute to overall stunting figures. <a href="/wiki/Iringa_Region" title="Iringa Region">Iringa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mbeya_Region" title="Mbeya Region">Mbeya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rukwa_Region" title="Rukwa Region">Rukwa</a> regions, where overall availability of food is considered acceptable, still experience stunting incidents in excess of 50%. In some areas where food shortages are common, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Tabora_Region" title="Tabora Region">Tabora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singida_Region" title="Singida Region">Singida</a> regions, stunting instances remain comparatively less than those seen in Iringa, Mbeya and Rukwa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-184">[181]</a></sup> The Tanzania Food and Nutrition Centre attributes these discrepancies to variance in maternal malnutrition, poor infant feeding practices, hygiene practices and poor healthcare services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-184">[181]</a></sup> Periods of drought can have significant impacts on the production of crops in Tanzania. Drought in East Africa has resulted in massive increases in the prices of food staples such as maize and <a href="/wiki/Commercial_sorghum" title="Commercial sorghum">sorghum</a>, crops crucial to the nutrition of the majority of Tanzania's population. From 2015 to 2017 the price of maize when bought wholesale more than tripled, from <a href="/wiki/Tanzanian_shilling" title="Tanzanian shilling">TSh</a>Â 400/<small><big>=</big></small> per kilogramme to 1,253/<small><big>=</big></small> per kilogramme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-185">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
