<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg/220px-Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg/330px-Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg/440px-Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Farmers using a rice harvester to harvest rice in Igunga District, Tanzania</div></div></div>
<p>Tanzania remains heavily agricultural, with 80% of the total population engaging in subsistence farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-:12-185">[182]</a></sup> Rural areas are subjected to increased food shortages in comparison to urbanised areas, with a survey carried out within the country in 2017 finding 84% of people in rural areas suffering food shortages over a 3-month period compared to 64% of residents in cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_185-2"><a href="#cite_note-:12-185">[182]</a></sup> This disparity between rural and city nutrition can be attributed to various factors; increased nutritional needs due to manual labour, more limited access to food as a result of poor infrastructure, high-susceptibility to the damaging effects of nature and the "Agricultural Productivity Gap".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[183]</a></sup> The Agricultural Productivity Gap postulates that "value added per worker" is often much lower within the agricultural sector than that found within non-agricultural sectors. Furthermore, allocation of labour within the agricultural sector is largely allocated ineffectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Programmes_targeting_hunger">Programmes targeting hunger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Programmes targeting hunger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>USAID programmes focusing on nutrition operate within the <a href="/wiki/Morogoro" title="Morogoro">Morogoro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dodoma" title="Dodoma">Dodoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iringa" title="Iringa">Iringa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mbeya" title="Mbeya">Mbeya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manyara_Region" title="Manyara Region">Manyara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Songwe_Region" title="Songwe Region">Songwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> regions of Tanzania. These "<a href="/wiki/Feed_the_Future_Initiative" title="Feed the Future Initiative">Feed the Future</a>" programmes heavily invest in nutrition, infrastructure, policy, capacity of institutions and agriculture which is identified by the organisation as a key area of economic growth in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-182">[179]</a></sup> A Tanzanian government led initiative "Kilimo Kwanza" or "Agriculture First" aims to encourage investment into agriculture within the private sector and hopes to improve agricultural processes and development within the country by seeking the knowledge of young people and the innovation that they can potentially provide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-188">[185]</a></sup> During the 1990s, around 25% of Tanzania's population were provided access to iodized oil aimed to target iodine deficiency within expecting mothers, as result of studies showing the negative effects of in-utero iodine deficiency on cognitive development in children. Research showed that children of mothers with access to the supplement achieved on average greater than a third of a year more education than those who did not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-188">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="437" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg/220px-Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg/330px-Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg/440px-Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of a World Food Programme parcel</div></div></div>
<p>Programmes led by the <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a> operate within Tanzania. The Supplementary Feeding Programme (SFP) aims to target acute malnutrition by supplying blended food fortified with vitamins to pregnant women and mothers to children under 5 on a monthly basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-189">[186]</a></sup> Pregnant women and mothers to children under 2 have access to the Mother and Child Health and Nutrition Programme's "Super Cereal" which is supplied with the intent of reducing stunting in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-189">[186]</a></sup> World Food Programme supplementation remains the main food source for Tanzania's refugees. Super Cereal, Vegetable Oil, Pulses and Salt are supplied as part of the Protracted Relief and Recovery Operation to meet the average person's minimum daily caloric requirement of 2,100 kcal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_189-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-189">[186]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> state that continued investment in nutrition within Tanzania is of the utmost importance: Estimates predict that Tanzania stands to lose $20 billion by 2025 if nutrition within the country remains at its current level, however improvements in nutrition could produce a gain of around $4.7 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-183">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Save the Children, with the help of UNICEF and <a href="/wiki/Irish_Aid" title="Irish Aid">Irish Aid</a> funding created the Partnership for Nutrition in Tanzania (PANITA), in 2011. PANITA aims to use civil society organisations to target nutrition within the country. Alongside this, various sectors associated with nutrition are targeted such as agriculture, water, sanitation, education, economic development and social progress. PANITA is responsible for ensuring significant attention is given to nutrition in development plans and budgets created on national and regional levels within Tanzania. Since its conception, PANITA has grown from 94 to 306 participating civil society organisations nationwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-190">[187]</a></sup> Agriculture within Tanzania is targeted by the Irish Aid led initiative Harnessing Agriculture for Nutrition Outcomes (HANO), which aims to merge nutrition initiatives with agriculture in the Lindi District of the country. The project aims to reduce stunting by 10% in children aged 0 to 23 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-190">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Science and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Researchers_(HC)_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants,_2013_or_closest_year.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="336" data-file-width="232" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg/220px-Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg/330px-Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg/440px-Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Researchers_(HC)_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants,_2013_or_closest_year.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Researchers (HC) in Southern Africa per million inhabitants, 2013 or closest year</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_Tanzania" title="Science and technology in Tanzania">Science and technology in Tanzania</a></div>
<p>Tanzania's first "National Science and Technology Policy" was adopted in 1996. The objective of the government's "Vision 2025" (1998) document was to "transform the economy into a strong, resilient and competitive one, buttressed by science and technology".
</p><p>Under the umbrella of the One UN Initiative, UNESCO and Tanzanian government departments and agencies formulated a series of proposals in 2008 for revising the "National Science and Technology Policy". The total reform budget of US$10Â million was financed from the One UN fund and other sources. UNESCO provided support for mainstreaming science, technology, and innovation into the new "National Growth and Poverty Reduction Strategy" for the mainland and Zanzibar namely, Mkukuta II and Mkuza II, including in the field of tourism.
</p><p>Tanzania's revised science policy was published in 2010. Entitled "National Research and Development Policy", it recognises the need to improve the process of prioritisation of research capacities, develop international co-operation in strategic areas of research and development, and improve planning for human resources. It also makes provisions for the establishment of a National Research Fund. This policy was, in turn, reviewed in 2012 and 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-191">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="176" data-file-width="511" decoding="async" height="76" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg/220px-Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg/330px-Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg/440px-Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>ScientificÂ publications per million inhabitants in SADC countries in 2014. Source: UNESCO Science Report (2015), data from Thomson Reuters' Web of Science, Science Citation Index Expanded</div></div></div>
<p>In 2010, Tanzania devoted 0.38 per cent of GDP to research and development. The global average in 2013 was 1.7 per cent of GDP. Tanzania had 69 researchers (in head counts) per million population in 2010. In 2014, Tanzania counted 15 publications per million inhabitants in internationally catalogued journals, according to Thomson Reuters' Web of Science (Science Citation Index Expanded). The average for sub-Saharan Africa was 20 publications per million inhabitants and the global average 176 publications per million inhabitants. Tanzania was ranked 90th in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index" title="Global Innovation Index">Global Innovation Index</a> in 2021, up from 97th in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Tanzania" title="Demographics of Tanzania">Demographics of Tanzania</a></div>
<table class="wikitable floatleft">
<caption>Population in Tanzania<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-196">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2022_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2022-197">[194]</a></sup>
</caption>

<td style="text-align:right;">63.6

<p>According to the 2012 census, the total population of Tanzania was 44,928,923.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012_census_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-2012_census-5">[5]</a></sup> The under-15 age group represented 44.1% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbs1_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-nbs1-198">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>The population distribution in Tanzania is significantly uneven. Most people live on the northern border or the coast, with much of the remainder of the country being sparsely populated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame_71-18"><a href="#cite_note-frame-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 1252">:âpage 1252â</span></sup> Density varies from 12 per square kilometre (31/sqÂ mi) in the <a href="/wiki/Katavi_Region" title="Katavi Region">Katavi Region</a> to 3,133 per square kilometre (8,110/sqÂ mi) in the <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam_Region" title="Dar es Salaam Region">Dar es Salaam Region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012_census_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-2012_census-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 6">:âpage 6â</span></sup>
</p><p>Approximately 70% of the population is rural, although this percentage has been declining since at least 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[196]</a></sup> Dar es Salaam (population 4,364,541)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census2012_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-census2012-200">[197]</a></sup> is the largest city and commercial capital. The capital of the country and economic centre of Tanzania, <a href="/wiki/Dodoma" title="Dodoma">Dodoma</a> (population 410,956)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census2012_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-census2012-200">[197]</a></sup> is located in central Tanzania, and hosts the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Tanzania)" title="National Assembly (Tanzania)">National Assembly</a>.
</p><p>At the time of the foundation of the United Republic of Tanzania in 1964, the child mortality rate was 335 deaths per 1,000 live births. Since independence, the rate of child deaths has declined to 62 per 1000 births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[198]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg/220px-Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg/330px-Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg/440px-Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Hadza_people" title="Hadza people">Hadza</a> live as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The population consists of <a href="/wiki/List_of_ethnic_groups_in_Tanzania" title="List of ethnic groups in Tanzania">about 125 ethnic groups</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev-202">[199]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sukuma_people" title="Sukuma people">Sukuma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyamwezi_people" title="Nyamwezi people">Nyamwezi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaga_people" title="Chaga people">Chagga</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Haya_people" title="Haya people">Haya</a> peoples each have a population exceeding 1 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 4">:âpage 4â</span></sup> Approximately 99 per cent of Tanzanians are of native <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_of_Africa" title="Ethnic groups of Africa">African</a> descent, with small numbers of Arab, European, and Asian descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev-202">[199]</a></sup> The majority of Tanzanians, including the Sukuma and the Nyamwezi, are <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>The population also includes people of Arab and <a href="/wiki/Indians_in_Tanzania" title="Indians in Tanzania">Indian</a> origin, and small European and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_Tanzania" title="Chinese people in Tanzania">Chinese</a> communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[202]</a></sup> Many also identify as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shirazi_(ethnic_group)" title="Shirazi (ethnic group)">Shirazis</a>. Thousands of Arabs and Indians were massacred during the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Revolution" title="Zanzibar Revolution">Zanzibar Revolution</a> of 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar-53">[51]</a></sup> As of 1994, the Asian community numbered 50,000 on the mainland and 4,000 on Zanzibar. An estimated 70,000 Arabs and 10,000 Europeans lived in Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pop_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-pop-206">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albinos" title="Albinos">albinos</a> in Tanzania have been the victims of violence in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[207]</a></sup> Attacks are often to hack off the limbs of albinos in the perverse superstitious belief that possessing the bones of albinos will bring wealth. The country has banned witch doctors to try to prevent the practice, but it has continued and albinos remain targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn091015_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn091015-211">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to 2010 Tanzanian government statistics, the <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rate</a> in Tanzania was 5.4 children born per woman, with 3.7 in urban mainland areas, 6.1 in rural mainland areas, and 5.1 in Zanzibar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DHS_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-DHS-212">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 55">:âpage 55â</span></sup> For all women aged 45â49, 37.3 per cent had given birth to eight or more children, and for currently married women in that age group, 45.0 per cent had given birth to that many children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DHS_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-DHS-212">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 61">:âpage 61â</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Tanzania" title="Religion in Tanzania">Religion in Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Tanzania" title="Christianity in Tanzania">Christianity in Tanzania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Zanzibar" title="Islam in Zanzibar">Islam in Zanzibar</a></div>
<div class="barbox tright" style="overflow-x: auto;">
<div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:250px; background: white;">
<table style="text-align:left; border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">
<tbody><tr style="background:none"><th colspan="5" style="text-align:center;">Religion in <b>Tanzania</b> (2014)</th></tr>
<tr style="font-size:88%; height:4px;">

</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a></td>

</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_African_religion" title="Traditional African religion">Indigenous beliefs</a></td>

</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">Other</a></td>
<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;"><div style="background:yellow; width:1.6%; overflow: hidden;">â</div></td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 1.2em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">1.6%</td>
</tr>
<tr><td colspan="5" style="padding:5px; text-align:left;">Source: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-15">[13]</a></sup></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
</div>
</div><p>Official statistics on religion are unavailable because religious surveys were eliminated from government census reports after 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[210]</a></sup> Tanzania's religious field is dominated by Christianity and Islam as well as of different African traditional religions connected to ethnic customs. The word for religion in Swahili, <i>dini,</i> generally apply to the world religions of Christianity and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> meaning that followers of traditional African religions are considered to be of "no religion". Religious belonging is often ambiguous, with some people adhering to multiple religious identities at the same time (for instance being Christian but also following African traditional rituals) something which suggests that religious boundaries are flexible and contextual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[211]</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stone_Town,_St._Joseph_Cathedral.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Stone_Town%2C_St._Joseph_Cathedral.jpg/220px-Stone_Town%2C_St._Joseph_Cathedral.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Stone_Town%2C_St._Joseph_Cathedral.jpg/330px-Stone_Town%2C_St._Joseph_Cathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Stone_Town%2C_St._Joseph_Cathedral.jpg/440px-Stone_Town%2C_St._Joseph_Cathedral.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stone_Town,_St._Joseph_Cathedral.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>St Joseph's Catholic cathedral, Zanzibar</div></div></div>
<p>According to a 2014 estimate by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a></i>, 61.4% of the population was Christian, 35.2% was Muslim, 1.8% practised traditional African religions, 1.4% were unaffiliated with any religion, and 0.2% followed other religions. Nearly the entire population of Zanzibar is Muslim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-15">[13]</a></sup> Of Muslims, 16% are <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a>, 20% are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-denominational_Muslims" title="Non-denominational Muslims">non-denominational Muslims</a>, 40% are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a>, 20 per cent are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Shia</a>, and 4% are <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within the Christian community the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> is the largest group (51% all Christians).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[213]</a></sup> Among the Protestants, the large number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutherans" title="Lutherans">Lutherans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravians</a> points to the German missionary past of the country, while the number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicans" title="Anglicans">Anglicans</a> point to the British missionary history of Tanganyika. A growing number have adopted <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostalism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adventists" title="Adventists">Adventists</a> likewise have an increasing presence because of external missionary activities from Scandinavia and the United States, especially during the first part of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[214]</a></sup> All of them have had some influence in varying degrees from the Walokole movement (<a href="/wiki/East_African_Revival" title="East African Revival">East African Revival</a>), which has also been fertile ground for the spread of <a href="/wiki/Charismatic_Christianity" title="Charismatic Christianity">charismatic</a> and Pentecostal groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are also active communities of other religious groups, primarily on the mainland, such as <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Tanzania" title="Hinduism in Tanzania">Hindus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith">BahÃ¡'Ã­s</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[216]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Tanzania" title="Languages of Tanzania">Languages of Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zanzibar_door_carved.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Zanzibar_door_carved.jpg/170px-Zanzibar_door_carved.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Zanzibar_door_carved.jpg/255px-Zanzibar_door_carved.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Zanzibar_door_carved.jpg/340px-Zanzibar_door_carved.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zanzibar_door_carved.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A carved door with Arabic calligraphy in Zanzibar</div></div></div>
<p>More than 100 <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Tanzania" title="Languages of Tanzania">languages are spoken in Tanzania</a>, making it the most linguistically diverse country in East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-sim-23">[21]</a></sup> Among the languages spoken are four of Africa's language families: <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cushitic_languages" title="Cushitic languages">Cushitic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nilotic_languages" title="Nilotic languages">Nilotic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">Khoisan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-sim-23">[21]</a></sup> There are no <i>de jure</i> official languages in Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tzgov_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-tzgov-26">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Swahili is used in parliamentary debate, in the lower courts, and as a medium of instruction in primary school. English is used in foreign trade, in diplomacy, in higher courts, and as a medium of instruction in secondary and higher education,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-sim-23">[21]</a></sup> The Tanzanian government, however, has plans to discontinue English as a language of instruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afkinsider.com_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-afkinsider.com-27">[25]</a></sup> In connection with his <a href="/wiki/Ujamaa" title="Ujamaa">Ujamaa</a> social policies, President Nyerere encouraged the use of Swahili to help unify the country's many ethnic groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joshua_A._Fishman_Distinguished_University_Research_Professor_of_Social_Sciences_Yeshiva_University_Emeritus_2001_361_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joshua_A._Fishman_Distinguished_University_Research_Professor_of_Social_Sciences_Yeshiva_University_Emeritus_2001_361-220">[217]</a></sup> Approximately 10 per cent of Tanzanians speak Swahili as a first language, and up to 90 per cent speak it as a second language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-sim-23">[21]</a></sup> Many educated Tanzanians are trilingual, also speaking English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quintin_Winks_2011_145_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quintin_Winks_2011_145-221">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bj_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-bj-222">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FranÃ§ois_Grosjean_1982_8_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FranÃ§ois_Grosjean_1982_8-223">[220]</a></sup> The widespread use and promotion of Swahili is contributing to the decline of smaller languages in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-sim-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthias_Brenzinger_1992_86_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matthias_Brenzinger_1992_86-224">[221]</a></sup> Young children increasingly speak Swahili as a first language, particularly in urban areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elsevier-225">[222]</a></sup> Ethnic community languages (ECL) other than Kiswahili are not allowed as a language of instruction. Nor are they taught as a subject, though they might be used unofficially in some cases in initial education. Television and radio programmes in an ECL are prohibited, and it is nearly impossible to get permission to publish a newspaper in an ECL. There is no department of local or regional African Languages and Literatures at the University of Dar es Salaam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> is co-official in <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sandawe_people" title="Sandawe people">Sandawe people</a> speak a language that may be related to the <a href="/wiki/Khoe_languages" title="Khoe languages">Khoe languages</a> of Botswana and Namibia, while the language of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadzabe_people" title="Hadzabe people">Hadzabe people</a>, although it has similar <a href="/wiki/Click_consonant" title="Click consonant">click consonants</a>, is arguably a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[224]</a></sup> The language of the <a href="/wiki/Iraqw_people" title="Iraqw people">Iraqw people</a> is Cushitic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[225]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_Libraries">Education and Libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Education and Libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Tanzania" title="Education in Tanzania">Education in Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nkrumah.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="2576" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Nkrumah.JPG/220px-Nkrumah.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Nkrumah.JPG/330px-Nkrumah.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Nkrumah.JPG/440px-Nkrumah.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nkrumah.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nkrumah Hall at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Dar_es_Salaam" title="University of Dar es Salaam">University of Dar es Salaam</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 2015, the literacy rate in Tanzania was 77.9% for people aged 15 and over (83.2% males, 73.1% females),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO_lit2015_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO_lit2015-229">[226]</a></sup> . Education is compulsory until children reach age 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worst_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-worst-230">[227]</a></sup> In 2020, 97% completed primary (98.4% females and 95.5% males), 28.3% completed secondary (30% females and 27% males), and 8% completed tertiary education (7% females and 8.5% males).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unesco_educatoin_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-unesco_educatoin-231">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Tanzania Library Services Board operates twenty-one regional, eighteen district, and one divisional library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[229]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Healthcare">Healthcare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Healthcare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Tanzania" title="Healthcare in Tanzania">Healthcare in Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Life_expectancy_in_Tanzania.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Life_expectancy_in_Tanzania.svg/250px-Life_expectancy_in_Tanzania.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Life_expectancy_in_Tanzania.svg/375px-Life_expectancy_in_Tanzania.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Life_expectancy_in_Tanzania.svg/500px-Life_expectancy_in_Tanzania.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Life_expectancy_in_Tanzania.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Development of life expectancy</div></div></div>
<p>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, life expectancy at birth was 61 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-who-233">[230]</a></sup> The under-five mortality rate in 2012 was 54 per 1,000 live births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-who-233">[230]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternal_mortality" title="Maternal mortality">maternal mortality</a> rate in 2013 was estimated at 410 per 100,000 live births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_233-2"><a href="#cite_note-who-233">[230]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premature_death" title="Premature death">Prematurity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> were tied in 2010 as the leading cause of death in children under 5 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_World_Health_Statistics_2013_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_World_Health_Statistics_2013-234">[231]</a></sup> The other leading causes of death for these children were, in decreasing order, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diarrhoea" title="Diarrhoea">diarrhoea</a>, HIV, and <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_World_Health_Statistics_2013_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_World_Health_Statistics_2013-234">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>Malaria in Tanzania causes death and disease and has a "huge economic impact".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Progress_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-Progress-235">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 13">:âpage 13â</span></sup> There were approximately 11.5Â million cases of clinical malaria in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Progress_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-Progress-235">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 12">:âpage 12â</span></sup> In 2007â08, malaria prevalence among children aged 6 months to 5 years was highest in the <a href="/wiki/Kagera_Region" title="Kagera Region">Kagera Region</a> (41.1 per cent) on the western shore of Lake Victoria and lowest in the <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Region" title="Arusha Region">Arusha Region</a> (0.1 per cent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Progress_235-2"><a href="#cite_note-Progress-235">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 12">:âpage 12â</span></sup>
</p><p>According to the 2010 <i>Tanzania Demographic and Health Survey 2010</i>, 15 per cent of Tanzanian women had undergone <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> (FGM)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DHS_212-2"><a href="#cite_note-DHS-212">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 295">:âpage 295â</span></sup> and 72 per cent of Tanzanian men had been circumcised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DHS_212-3"><a href="#cite_note-DHS-212">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 230">:âpage 230â</span></sup> FGM is most common in the <a href="/wiki/Manyara_Region" title="Manyara Region">Manyara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dodoma_Region" title="Dodoma Region">Dodoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Region" title="Arusha Region">Arusha</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Singida_Region" title="Singida Region">Singida</a> regions and nonexistent in <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DHS_212-4"><a href="#cite_note-DHS-212">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 296">:âpage 296â</span></sup> The prevalence of male circumcision was above 90 per cent in the eastern<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn091015_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-cnn091015-211">[208]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam_Region" title="Dar es Salaam Region">Dar es Salaam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pwani_Region" title="Pwani Region">Pwani</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Morogoro_Region" title="Morogoro Region">Morogoro</a> regions), northern (<a href="/wiki/Kilimanjaro_Region" title="Kilimanjaro Region">Kilimanjaro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanga_Region" title="Tanga Region">Tanga</a>, Arusha, and Manyara regions), and central areas (Dodoma and <a href="/wiki/Singida_Region" title="Singida Region">Singida</a> regions) and below 50 per cent only in the southern highlands zone (<a href="/wiki/Mbeya_Region" title="Mbeya Region">Mbeya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iringa_Region" title="Iringa Region">Iringa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rukwa_Region" title="Rukwa Region">Rukwa</a> regions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DHS_212-5"><a href="#cite_note-DHS-212">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: pages 6, 230">:âpages 6,â230â</span></sup>
</p><p>2012 data showed that 53 per cent of the population used improved drinking water sources (defined as a source that "by nature of its construction and design, is likely to protect the source from outside contamination, in particular from faecal matter") and 12 per cent used improved sanitation facilities (defined as facilities that "likely hygienically separates human excreta from human contact" but not including facilities shared with other households or open to public use).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[233]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tanzanian_tea_farmers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="1170" decoding="async" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Tanzanian_tea_farmers.jpg/300px-Tanzanian_tea_farmers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Tanzanian_tea_farmers.jpg/450px-Tanzanian_tea_farmers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Tanzanian_tea_farmers.jpg/600px-Tanzanian_tea_farmers.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tanzanian_tea_farmers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tanzanian women harvesting tea leaves</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Tanzania" title="Women in Tanzania">Women in Tanzania</a></div>
<p>Women and men have equality before the law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup>
The government signed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Discrimination_against_Women" title="Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination against Women">Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination against Women</a> (CEDAW) in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-1"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup>
Nearly 3 out of ten females reported having experienced <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence" title="Sexual violence">sexual violence</a> before the age of 18.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-2"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup> The prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> has decreased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-3"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup>
School girls are reinstated back to school after delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-4"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup>
The Police Force administration strives to separate the Gender Desks from normal police operations to enhance confidentiality of the processing of women victims of abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-5"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup>
Most of the abuses and violence against women and children occurs at the family level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-6"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Tanzania" title="Constitution of Tanzania">Constitution of Tanzania</a> requires that women constitute at least 30% of all elected members of <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Tanzania)" title="National Assembly (Tanzania)">National Assembly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-7"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup> The gender differences in education and training have implications later in life of these women and girls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-8"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup>
Unemployment is higher for females than for males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-9"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup> The right of a female employee to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternity_leave" title="Maternity leave">maternity leave</a> is guaranteed in labour law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedaw_237-10"><a href="#cite_note-cedaw-237">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Tanzania" title="Culture of Tanzania">Culture of Tanzania</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Tanzania" title="Music of Tanzania">Music of Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ngoma_Tanzania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Ngoma_Tanzania.jpg/220px-Ngoma_Tanzania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Ngoma_Tanzania.jpg/330px-Ngoma_Tanzania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Ngoma_Tanzania.jpg/440px-Ngoma_Tanzania.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ngoma_Tanzania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tanzanian Ngoma group</div></div></div>
<p>As in other countries, the musical in Tanzania is constantly undergoing changes, and varies by location, people, settings and occasion. The five music genres in Tanzania, as defined by <a href="/wiki/Baraza_la_Sanaa_la_Taifa" title="Baraza la Sanaa la Taifa">BASATA</a> are, <a href="/wiki/Ngoma_music" title="Ngoma music">ngoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muziki_wa_dansi" title="Muziki wa dansi">dansi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Choir" title="Choir">kwaya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taarab" title="Taarab">taarab</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Bongo_Flava" title="Bongo Flava">bongo flava</a> being added in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Askew_02_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Askew_02-238">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ivaska_11_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ivaska_11-239">[236]</a></sup> Singeli has since the mid 2000s been an unofficial music of <i>uswahilini</i>, unplanned communities in <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a>, and is the newest mainstream genre since 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson_18_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson_18-240">[237]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ngoma <i>(Bantu, meaning dance, drum and event)</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stone_08_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stone_08-241">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaduma_78_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaduma_78-242">[239]</a></sup> is a traditional dance music that has been the most widespread music in Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Njogu_07_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Njogu_07-243">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edmondson_07_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edmondson_07-244">[241]</a></sup> Dansi is urban jazz or band music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Njogu_07_243-1"><a href="#cite_note-Njogu_07-243">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahenge_22_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahenge_22-245">[242]</a></sup> Taarab is sung <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Kiswahili</a> poetry accompanied by a band, typically string, in which audience is often, but not always, encouraged to dance and clap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Njogu_07_243-2"><a href="#cite_note-Njogu_07-243">[240]</a></sup> Kwaya is choir music originally limited to church during colonization, but now an secular part of education, social, and political events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stone_08_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stone_08-241">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edmondson_07_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edmondson_07-244">[241]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bongo flava is Tanzanian pop music originating in the early 2000s from <i>muziki wa kizazi kipya</i>, meaning "Music of the new generation", which originated in the late 1980s. Kizazi kipya's dominant influences were <a href="/wiki/Reggae" title="Reggae">reggae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_and_blues" title="Rhythm and blues">RnB</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop</a>, where as the later bongo flava's dominant influences are taarab and dansi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerr_18_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kerr_18-246">[243]</a></sup> Three recent influence on bongo flava are <a href="/wiki/African_popular_music" title="African popular music">Afropop</a> in the 2010s, as well as <a href="/wiki/Amapiano" title="Amapiano">amapiano</a> from <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> and singeli from Tanzania, both since 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suriano_11_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suriano_11-247">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perullo_05_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perullo_05-248">[245]</a></sup> Singeli is a ngoma music that originated in Manzese, a uswahilini in north-west Dar es Salaam. A MC performs over fast tempo taarab music, often at between 200-300 <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">beets per minute (BPM)</a> while females dance. Styles between MC gender typically differ significantly. Male MCs usually perform in fast paced rap, while female MCs usually perform kwaya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson_18_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson_18-240">[237]</a></sup>
</p><p>From independence until 1993, all recording and distribution of music was strictly managed by BASATA, primarily through <a href="/wiki/Radio_Tanzania_Dar_es_Salaam" title="Radio Tanzania Dar es Salaam">Radio Tanzania Dar es Salaam</a> (RTD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perullo_07_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perullo_07-249">[246]</a></sup> Only the 4 Tanzanian genres were permitted to be recorded or broadcast, which at the time was ngoma, taarab, kwaya and dansi. The Broadcasting Services Act of 1993 allowed private broadcast networks and recording studios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[248]</a></sup> In the few years prior to the 1993 Act hip hop had been getting somewhat established in Dar es Salaam, Arusha and Mwanza. It was transitioning from English performances of hip hop originating in uzunguni, rich areas like Oysterbay and Masaki with international schools, to Kiswahili performances of kizazi kipya, originating in uswahilini<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saleh_J_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saleh_J-252">[249]</a></sup> Following the opening of the radio waves, bongo flava spreading through out the country, and the rest of the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerr_18_246-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kerr_18-246">[243]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="National_anthem">National anthem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: National anthem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>

<div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Tanzanian_national_anthem,_performed_by_the_United_States_Navy_Band.oga" title="File:Tanzanian national anthem, performed by the United States Navy Band.oga">National Anthem of Tanzania</a></div>

<div class="description">National Anthem of Tanzania performed by United States Navy Band<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[250]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>The Tanzanian national anthem is "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mungu_Ibariki_Africa" title="Mungu Ibariki Africa">Mungu Ibariki Africa</a>" (God Bless Africa). It has kiswahili lyrics adapted for "<a href="/wiki/Nkosi_Sikelel%27_iAfrika" title="Nkosi Sikelel' iAfrika">Nkosi Sikelel' iAfrika</a>" composed by South African composer <a href="/wiki/Enoch_Sontonga" title="Enoch Sontonga">Enoch Sontonga</a> in 1897.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[251]</a></sup> "Nkosi Sikelel' iAfrika" became a pan-African song adapted into the current national anthems for Tanzania, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, and following the end of apartheid <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millennium_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millennium-255">[252]</a></sup> It was previously used in the national anthems for <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, but has since been replaced by original new anthems. Another patriotic song is <a href="/wiki/Tanzania_Nakupenda_Kwa_Moyo_Wote" title="Tanzania Nakupenda Kwa Moyo Wote">Tanzania, Tanzania</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tanzanian_literature" title="Tanzanian literature">Tanzanian literature</a></div>
<p>Tanzania's literary culture is primarily oral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 68">:âpage 68â</span></sup> Major oral literary forms include folktales, poems, riddles, proverbs, and songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-2"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 69">:âpage 69â</span></sup> The greatest part of Tanzania's recorded oral literature is in Swahili, even though each of the country's languages has its own oral tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-3"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: pages 68â9">:âpages 68â9â</span></sup> The country's oral literature has been declining because of the breakdown of the multigenerational social structure, making transmission of oral literature more difficult, and because increasing modernisation has been accompanied by the devaluation of oral literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-4"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 69">:âpage 69â</span></sup>
</p><p>Books in Tanzania are often expensive and hard to come by.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-5"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 75">:âpage 75â</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doling_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doling-256">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 16">:âpage 16â</span></sup> Most Tanzanian literature is in Swahili or English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-6"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: page 75">:âpage 75â</span></sup> Major figures in Tanzanian written literature include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaaban_Robert" title="Shaaban Robert">Shaaban Robert</a> (considered the father of Swahili literature), Muhammed Saley Farsy, Faraji Katalambulla, Adam Shafi Adam, <a href="/wiki/Muhammed_Said_Abdulla" title="Muhammed Said Abdulla">Muhammed Said Abdalla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_K._Palangyo" title="Peter K. Palangyo">Peter K. Palangyo</a>, Said Ahmed Mohammed Khamis, Mohamed Suleiman Mohamed, <a href="/wiki/Euphrase_Kezilahabi" title="Euphrase Kezilahabi">Euphrase Kezilahabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Ruhumbika" title="Gabriel Ruhumbika">Gabriel Ruhumbika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ebrahim_Hussein" title="Ebrahim Hussein">Ebrahim Hussein</a>, <a href="/wiki/May_Balisidya" title="May Balisidya">May Materru Balisidya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fadhy_Mtanga" title="Fadhy Mtanga">Fadhy Mtanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdulrazak_Gurnah" title="Abdulrazak Gurnah">Abdulrazak Gurnah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Penina_Muhando" title="Penina Muhando">Penina O. Mlama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-7"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: pages 76â8">:âpages 76â8â</span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amani-TT4798.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="515" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Amani-TT4798.jpg/220px-Amani-TT4798.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Amani-TT4798.jpg/330px-Amani-TT4798.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Amani-TT4798.jpg/440px-Amani-TT4798.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amani-TT4798.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Tingatinga_(painting)" title="Tingatinga (painting)">Tingatinga</a> painting</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Painting_and_sculpture">Painting and sculpture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Painting and sculpture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two Tanzanian art styles have achieved international recognition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doling_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-Doling-256">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p. 17">:âp. 17â</span></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tingatinga_(painting)" title="Tingatinga (painting)">Tingatinga</a> school of painting, founded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Said_Tingatinga" title="Edward Said Tingatinga">Edward Said Tingatinga</a>, consists of brightly coloured enamel paintings on canvas, generally depicting people, animals, or daily life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-8"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p. 113">:âp. 113â</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doling_256-2"><a href="#cite_note-Doling-256">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p. 17">:âp. 17â</span></sup> After Tingatinga's death in 1972, other artists adopted and developed his style, with the genre now being the most important tourist-oriented style in East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Otiso_203-9"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p. 113">:âp. 113â</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doling_256-3"><a href="#cite_note-Doling-256">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p. 17">:âp. 17â</span></sup>
</p><p>Historically, there were limited opportunities for formal European art training in Tanzania and many aspiring Tanzanian artists left the country to pursue their vocation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doling_256-4"><a href="#cite_note-Doling-256">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p. 17">:âp. 17â</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Tanzania" title="Sport in Tanzania">Sport in Tanzania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tanzania_National_Main_Stadium_Aerial.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2034" data-file-width="3490" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Tanzania_National_Main_Stadium_Aerial.jpg/220px-Tanzania_National_Main_Stadium_Aerial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Tanzania_National_Main_Stadium_Aerial.jpg/330px-Tanzania_National_Main_Stadium_Aerial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Tanzania_National_Main_Stadium_Aerial.jpg/440px-Tanzania_National_Main_Stadium_Aerial.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tanzania_National_Main_Stadium_Aerial.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/National_Stadium_(Tanzania)" title="National Stadium (Tanzania)">National Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Football</a> is very popular throughout the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wairagala_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wairagala-257">[254]</a></sup> The most popular <a href="/wiki/Tanzanian_Premier_League" title="Tanzanian Premier League">professional football clubs</a> in Dar es Salaam are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Young_Africans_F.C." title="Young Africans F.C.">Young Africans F.C.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simba_S.C." title="Simba S.C.">Simba S.C.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[255]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tanzania_Football_Federation" title="Tanzania Football Federation">Tanzania Football Federation</a> is the governing body for football in the country.
</p><p>Other popular sports include <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netball" title="Netball">netball</a>, boxing, <a href="/wiki/Volleyball" title="Volleyball">volleyball</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athletics_(sport)" title="Athletics (sport)">athletics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wairagala_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wairagala-257">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[256]</a></sup> The National Sports Council also known as Baraza la Michezo la Taifa is the governing body for sports in the country under the Ministry of Information, Youth, Sports and Culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[257]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema">Cinema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Cinema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tanzania has a popular film industry known as "Bongo Movie". The music industry is known as "<a href="/wiki/Bongo_Flava" title="Bongo Flava">Bongo Flava</a>" which is in itself also a niche genre of music in Tanzania.
</p>
